10085-8{_}1538{_}"Lady," he said, "remember well   That Moor of purpose fierce and fell   On whom my vengeance I did wreak   Hast felt the curse that now you speak.
10085-8{_}1539{_}And as for Zaida, I repent   That love of mine on her was spent.
10085-8{_}1540{_}Disdain of her and love of thee   Now rule my soul in company.
10085-8{_}1541{_}The flame in which for her I burned   To frost her cruelty has turned.
10085-8{_}1542{_}Three cursed years, to win her smile,     In knightly deeds I wrought,   And nothing but her treachery     My faithful service brought,   She flung me off without a qualm,     Because my lot was poor,   And gave, because the wretch was rich,     Her favor to a Moor."
10085-8{_}1543{_}Celinda as these words she heard   Impatiently the lattice barred,   And to the lover's ardent sight   It seemed that heaven was quenched in night.
10085-8{_}1544{_}A page came riding up the street,   Bringing the knight his jennets fleet,   With plumes and harness all bedight   And saddled well with housings bright;   The lance which he on entering bore   Brandished the knight with spirit sore,     And dashed it to the wall,   And head and butt, at that proud door,     In myriad fragments fall.
10085-8{_}1545{_}He bade them change from green to gray;     The plumes and harness borne that day   By all the coursers of his train.
10085-8{_}1546{_}In rage disconsolate,   He rode from Gelva, nor drew rein     Up to Sanlucar's gate.
10085-8{_}1547{_}VENGEANCE OF GAZUL    Not Rodamont the African,     The ruler of Argel,   And King of Zarza's southern coast,     Was filled with rage so fell,   When for his darling Doralice     He fought with Mandricard,   As filled the heart of bold Gazul     When, past Sidonia's guard,   He sallied forth in arms arrayed,     With courage high prepared   To do a deed that mortal man     Never before had dared.
10085-8{_}1548{_}It was for this he bade them bring     His barb and coat of mail;   A sword and dusky scabbard     'Neath his left shoulder trail;   In Fez a Christian captive     Had forged it, laboring   At arms of subtile temper     As bondsman of the King.
10085-8{_}1549{_}More precious 'twas to bold Gazul     Than all his realms could bring.
10085-8{_}1550{_}A tawny tinted _alquizel_     Beneath his arms he wore;   And, to conceal his thoughts of blood,     No towering spear he bore.
10085-8{_}1551{_}He started forth for Jerez,     And hastening on his course,   Trampled the vega far and wide     With hoof-prints of his horse.
10085-8{_}1552{_}And soon he crossed the splashing ford     Of Guadelate's tide,   Hard by the ancient haven     Upon the valley-side.
10085-8{_}1553{_}They gave the ford a famous name     The waters still retain,   Santa Maria was it called,     Since Christians conquered Spain.
10085-8{_}1554{_}The river crossed, he spurred his steed,     Lest he might reach the gate   Of Jarez at an hour unfit,     Too early or too late.
10085-8{_}1555{_}For Zaida, his own Zaida,     Had scorned her lover leal,   Wedding a rich and potent Moor     A native of Seville;   The nephew of a castellan,     A Moorish prince of power,   Who in Seville was seneschal     Of castle and of tower.
10085-8{_}1556{_}By this accursed bridal     Life's treasure he had lost;   The Moor had gained the treasure,     And now must pay the cost.
10085-8{_}1557{_}The second hour of night had rung     When, on his gallant steed,   He passed thro' Jerez' gate resolved     Upon a desperate deed.
10085-8{_}1558{_}And lo!
10085-8{_}1559{_}to Zaida's dwelling     With peaceful mien he came,   Pondering his bloody vengeance     Upon that house of shame.
10085-8{_}1560{_}For he will pass the portal,     And strike the bridegroom low;   But first must cross the wide, wide court,     Ere he can reach his foe.
10085-8{_}1561{_}And he must pass the crowd of men,     Who in the courtyard stand,   Lighting the palace of the Moor,     With torches in their hand.
10085-8{_}1562{_}And Zaida in the midst comes forth,     Her lover at her side;   He has come, amid his groomsmen,     To take her for his bride.
10085-8{_}1563{_}And bold Gazul feels his heart bound     With fury at the sight;   A lion's rage is in his soul,     His brow is black as night.
10085-8{_}1564{_}But now he checks his anger,     And gently on his steed   Draws near, with smile of greeting,     That none may balk the deed.
10085-8{_}1565{_}And when he reached the bridal,     Where all had taken their stand,   Upon his mighty sword-hilt     He sudden laid his hand;   And in a voice that all could hear     "Base craven Moor," said he,   "The sweet, the lovely Zaida     Shall ne'er be bride to thee.
10085-8{_}1566{_}And count me not a traitor, I     Defy thee face to face,   Lay hand upon thy scimitar     If thou hast heart of grace."
10085-8{_}1567{_}And with these words he dealt one stroke,     A cruel stroke and true,   It reached the Moor, it struck his heart     And pierced it through and through.
10085-8{_}1568{_}Down fell the wretch, that single stroke     Had laid him with the dead--   "Now let him die for all his deeds,"     The assembled people said.
10085-8{_}1569{_}Gazul made bravely his defence,     And none could check his flight;   He dashed his rowels in his steed,     And vanished in the night.
10085-8{_}1570{_}GAZUL AND ALBENZAIDE    "Tho' thou the lance can hurl as well     As one a reed might cast,   Talk not of courage for thy crimes     Thy house's honor blast.
10085-8{_}1571{_}Seek not the revel or the dance,     Loved by each Moorish dame.
10085-8{_}1572{_}The name of valor is not thine,     Thou hast a coward's name;   And lay aside thy mantle fair     Thy veil and gaberdine,   And boast no more of gold and gems--     Thou hast disgraced thy line.
10085-8{_}1573{_}And see thine arms, for honor fit,     Are cheap and fashioned plain;   Yet such that he whose name is lost     May win it back again.
10085-8{_}1574{_}And Albenzaide keep thy tastes     Proportioned to thy state;   For oft from unrestrained desires     Spring hopes infatuate.
10085-8{_}1575{_}Flee from thy thoughts, for they have wings,     Whose light ambition lifts   Thy soul to empty altitudes,     Where purpose veers and drifts.
10085-8{_}1576{_}Fling not thyself into the sea,     From which the breezes blow   Now with abrupt disdain, and now     With flattering whispers low.
10085-8{_}1577{_}For liberty once forfeited     Is hard to be regained,   And hardest, when the forfeit falls     On heart and hand unstained."
10085-8{_}1578{_}Thus spake Gazul, the Moorish lord     Of fame and honor bright;   Yet, as a craven beggar,     Fair Zaida scorned the knight.
10085-8{_}1579{_}GAZUL'S ARMS    "Now scour for me my coat of mail,     Without delay, my page,   For, so grief's fire consumes me,     Thy haste will be an age;   And take from out my bonnet     The verdant plumes of pride,   Which once Azarco gave me,     When he took to him his bride.
10085-8{_}1580{_}And in their place put feathers black,     And write this motto there:   'Heavy as lead is now his heart,     Oppressed with a leaden care,'   And take away the diamonds,     And in their place insert   Black gems, that shall to all proclaim     The deed that does me hurt,   For if thou take away those gems     It will announce to all   The black and dismal lot that does     Unfortuned me befall.
10085-8{_}1581{_}And give to me the buskins plain,     Decked by no jewels' glow,   For he to whom the world is false     Had best in mourning go.
10085-8{_}1582{_}And give to me my lance of war,     Whose point is doubly steeled,   And, by the blood of Christians,     Was tempered in the field.
10085-8{_}1583{_}For well I wish my goodly blade     Once more may burnished glow;   And if I can to cleave in twain     The body of my foe.
10085-8{_}1584{_}And hang upon my baldric,     The best of my ten swords.
10085-8{_}1585{_}Black as the midnight is the sheath,     And with the rest accords.
10085-8{_}1586{_}Bring me the horse the Christian slave     Gave to me for his sire,   At Jaen; and no ransom     But that did I require.
10085-8{_}1587{_}And even though he be not shod,     Make haste to bring him here;   Though treachery from men I dread,     From beasts I have no fear.
10085-8{_}1588{_}The straps with rich enamel decked     I bid you lay aside;   And bind the rowels to my heel     With thongs of dusky hide."
10085-8{_}1589{_}Thus spake aloud the brave Gazul,     One gloomy Tuesday night;   Gloomy the eve, as he prepared     For victory in the fight.
10085-8{_}1590{_}For on that day the news had come     That his fair Moorish maid   Had wedded with his bitterest foe,     The hated Albenzaide.
10085-8{_}1591{_}The Moor was rich and powerful,     But not of lineage high,   His wealth outweighed with one light maid     Three years of constancy.
10085-8{_}1592{_}Touched to the heart, on hearing this,     He stood in arms arrayed,   Nor strange that he, disarmed by love,     'Gainst love should draw his blade.
10085-8{_}1593{_}And Venus, on the horizon,     Had shown her earliest ray   When he Sidonia left, and straight     To Jerez took his way.
10085-8{_}1594{_}THE TOURNAMENT    His temples glittered with the spoils and garlands of his love,   When stout Gazul to Gelvas came, the jouster's skill to prove.
10085-8{_}1595{_}He rode a fiery dappled gray, like wind he scoured the plain;   Yet all her power and mettle could a slender bit restrain;   The livery of his pages was purple, green, and red--   Tints gay as was the vernal joy within his bosom shed.
10085-8{_}1596{_}And all had lances tawny gray, and all on jennets rode,   Plumes twixt their ears; adown their flanks the costly housings flowed.
10085-8{_}1597{_}Himself upon his gallant steed carries the circling shield,   And a new device is blazoned upon its ample field.
10085-8{_}1598{_}The phoenix there is figured, on flaming nest it dies,   And from its dust and ashes again it seems to rise.
10085-8{_}1599{_}And on the margin of the shield this motto is expressed:   "Tis hard to hide the flames of love once kindled in the breast."
10085-8{_}1600{_}And now the ladies take their seats; each jouster mounts his steed;   From footmen and from horsemen flies fast the loaded reed.
10085-8{_}1601{_}And there appears fair Zaida, whom in a luckless day   The Moor had loved, but since, that love in loathing passed away.
10085-8{_}1602{_}Her treachery had grieved his heart, and she who did the wrong   Mourned with repentant heart amid that gay and happy throng.
10085-8{_}1603{_}And with her was Zafira, to whom her husband brings   More bliss and happiness than reign amid Granada's kings.
10085-8{_}1604{_}And when she looked at brave Gazul his deeds her grief renew;   The more she sees, the more her heart is ravished at the view.
10085-8{_}1605{_}And now she blushes with desire, now grows with envy pale;   Her heart is like the changing beam that quivers in the scale.
10085-8{_}1606{_}Alminda sees the lovely dame with sudden anguish start,   And speaks with hope she may reveal the secret of her heart.
10085-8{_}1607{_}And troubled Zaida makes reply, "A sudden thought of ill   Has flashed across my mind and caused the anguish that I feel."
10085-8{_}1608{_}"'Twere better," said Alminda, "to check thy fancy's flight,   For thought can rob the happiest hours of all their deep delight."
10085-8{_}1609{_}Then said the maid of Xerez, "To me thou showest plain   Thou hast not felt black envy's tooth nor known what is disdain.
10085-8{_}1610{_}To know it, would thy spirit move to pity my despair,   Who writhe and die from agony, in which thou hast no share."
10085-8{_}1611{_}Zafira seized the lady's hand, and silence fell around,   As mixed in loud confusion brushed the jousters to the ground.
10085-8{_}1612{_}In came the Berber tribesmen, in varied cloaks arrayed;   They ranged themselves in companies against the palisade.
10085-8{_}1613{_}The sound of barbarous trumpets rang, the startled horses reared,   And snort and neigh and tramp of hoofs on every side was heard,   Then troop meets troop, and valiant hearts the mimic fight pursue;   They hurl their javelins o'er the sand and pierce the bucklers through.
10085-8{_}1614{_}Long time the battling hosts contend, until that festive day,   The shout, the clash, the applauding cry, in silence die away.
10085-8{_}1615{_}They fain had prayed that time himself would stop Apollo's car.
10085-8{_}1616{_}They hate to see the sunset gloom, the rise of evening's star.
10085-8{_}1617{_}And even when the sun is set, he who a foe discerns,   With no less vigor to his targe the loaded javelin turns,   The onset joined, each lance discharged, the judge's voice is heard;   He bids the heralds sound a truce, and the wide lists are cleared.
10085-8{_}1618{_}ABENUMEYA'S LAMENT    The young Abenumeya, Granada's royal heir,   Was brave in battle with his foe and gallant with the fair.
10085-8{_}1619{_}By lovely Felisarda his heart had been ensnared,   The daughter of brave Ferri; the captain of the guard.
10085-8{_}1620{_}He through the vega of Genii bestrode his sorrel steed,   Alone, on melancholy thoughts his anxious soul to feed,   The tints that clothed the landscape round were gloomy as the scene   Of his past life, wherein his lot had naught but suffering been.
10085-8{_}1621{_}His mantle hue was of iron gray bestrewn with purple flowers,   Which bloomed amid distress and pain, like hope of happier hours.
10085-8{_}1622{_}And on his cloak were columns worked, (his cloak was saffron hued,)   To show that dark suspicion's fears had tried his fortitude;   His shield was blazoned with the moon, a purple streak above,   To show that fears of fickleness are ever born with love.
10085-8{_}1623{_}He bore an azure pennant 'neath the iron of his spear,   To show that lovers oft go wrong deceived by jealous fear.
10085-8{_}1624{_}The hood he wore was wrought of gold and silk of crimson clear;   His bonnet crest was a heron plume with an emerald stone beneath;   And under all a motto ran, "Too long a hope is death."
10085-8{_}1625{_}He started forth in such array, but armed from head to heel   With tempered blade and dagger and coat of twisted steel.
10085-8{_}1626{_}And hangling low at his saddle-bow was the helmet for his head;   And as he journeyed on his way the warrior sighed and said:   "O Felisarda, dearest maid, him in thy memory keep   Who in his soul has writ thy name in letters dark and deep.
10085-8{_}1627{_}Think that for thee in coat of mail he ever rides afield,   In his right hand the spear must stand, his left must grasp the shield.
10085-8{_}1628{_}And he must skirmish in the plain and broil of battle brave,   And wounded be, for weapons ne'er from jealousy can save."
10085-8{_}1629{_}And as he spoke the lonely Moor from out his mantle's fold   With many a sigh, that scorched the air, a lettered page unrolled.
10085-8{_}1630{_}He tried in vain to read it but his eyes with tears were blind,   And mantling clouds of sorrow hid the letters from his mind.
10085-8{_}1631{_}The page was moistened by the tears that flowed in plenteous tide,   But by the breath of sighs and sobs the softened page was dried.
10085-8{_}1632{_}Fresh wounds he felt at sight of it, and when the cause he sought,   His spirit to Granada flew upon the wings of thought.
10085-8{_}1633{_}He thought of Albaicin, the palace of the dame,   With its gayly gilded capitals and its walls of ancient fame.
10085-8{_}1634{_}And the garden that behind it lay in which the palm was seen   Swaying beneath the load of fruit its coronet of green.
10085-8{_}1635{_}"O mistress of my soul," he said, "who callest me thine own,   How easily all bars to bliss thy love might trample down!
10085-8{_}1636{_}But time, that shall my constancy, thy fickleness will show,   The world shall then my steadfast heart, thy tongue of treachery know.
10085-8{_}1637{_}Woe worth the day when, for thy sake, I fair Granada sought,   These anxious doubts may cloud my brow, they cannot guard thy thought.
10085-8{_}1638{_}My foes increase, thy cruelty makes absence bitterer still,   But naught can shake my constancy, and none can do me ill."   On this from Alpujarra the tocsin sounded high.
10085-8{_}1639{_}He rushed as one whose life is staked to save the maid or die.
10085-8{_}1640{_}THE DESPONDENT LOVER    He leaned upon his sabre's hilt,     He trod upon his shield,   Upon the ground he threw the lance     That forced his foes to yield.
10085-8{_}1641{_}His bridle hung at saddle-bow,     And, with the reins close bound,   His mare the garden entered free     To feed and wander round.
10085-8{_}1642{_}Upon a flowering almond-tree     He fixed an ardent gaze;   Its leaves were withered with the wind     That flowers in ruin lays.
10085-8{_}1643{_}Thus in Toledo's garden park,     Did Abenamar wait,   Who for fair Galliana     Watched at the palace gate.
10085-8{_}1644{_}The birds that clustered on the towers     Spread out their wings to fly,   And from afar his lady's veil     He saw go floating by.
10085-8{_}1645{_}And at this vision of delight,     Which healed his spirit's pain,   The exiled Moor took courage,     And hope returned again.
10085-8{_}1646{_}"O Galliana, best beloved,     Whom art thou waiting now?
10085-8{_}1647{_}And what has treacherous rendered     My fortune and thy vow?
10085-8{_}1648{_}Thou swearedst I should be thine own,     Yet 'twas but yesterday   We met, and with no greeting     Thou wentest on thy way.
10085-8{_}1649{_}Then, in my silence of distress,     I wandered pondering--   If this is what to-day has brought,     What will to-morrow bring?
10085-8{_}1650{_}Happy the Moor from passion free,     In peace or turmoil born,   Who without pang of hate or love,     Can slumber till the morn.
10085-8{_}1651{_}O almond-tree, thou provest     That the expected hours   Of bliss may often turn to bane,     As fade thy dazzling flowers.
10085-8{_}1652{_}A mournful image art thou     Of all that lays me low,   And on my shield I'll bear thee     As blazon of my woe.
10085-8{_}1653{_}For thou dost bloom in many a flower,     Till blasted by the wind,   And 'tis of thee this word is true--     'The season was not kind.'"
10085-8{_}1654{_}He spoke and on his courser's head     He slipped the bridle rein,   And while he curbed his gentle steed     He could not curb his pain,   And to Ocana took his course,     O'er Tagus' verdant plain.
10085-8{_}1655{_}LOVE AND JEALOUSY    "Unless thou wishest in one hour     Thine April hope shouldst blighted be,   Oh, tell me, Tarfe, tell me true,     How I may Zaida chance to see.
10085-8{_}1656{_}I mean the foreigner, the wife     New wedded, her with golden hair,   And for each lock a charm besides     She counts--for she is passing fair.
10085-8{_}1657{_}Her, whom the Moorish nobles all     To heaven in their laudation raise,   Till the fine ladies of the land     Are left to languish in dispraise.
10085-8{_}1658{_}The mosque I visit every day,     And wait to see her come in sight;   I wait to see her, where the rout     And revel lengthen out the night.
10085-8{_}1659{_}However, cost me what it may,     I cannot meet the lovely dame.
10085-8{_}1660{_}Ah, now my eyes are veiled in tears,     Sure witness of my jealous flame.
10085-8{_}1661{_}And tell me, Tarfe, that my rage     Has cause enough, for since I've been   Granada's guest (and would to God     Granada I had never seen!)
10085-8{_}1662{_}My lord forsakes me every night,     Nor till the morning comes again;   He shuns as painful my caress,     My very presence brings him pain;   Little indeed he recks of me,     If only he may elsewhere reign.
10085-8{_}1663{_}For if we in the garden meet,     Or if we in the chamber be,   His actions his estrangement prove,     He has not even words for me.
10085-8{_}1664{_}And if I say to him, 'My life!'
10085-8{_}1665{_}He answers me, 'My dearest dear,'   Yet with a coldness that congeals     My very heart with sudden fear.
10085-8{_}1666{_}And all the while I strive to make     His soul reveal a traitorous thought,   He turns his back on me, as if     To him my trembling fear was naught.
10085-8{_}1667{_}And when about his neck I cling,     He drops his eyes and bows his face,   As if, from thought of other arms     He longed to slip from my embrace.
10085-8{_}1668{_}His bosom heaves with discontent,     Deep as from hell the sigh is wrenched;   My heart with dark suspicion beats,     And all my happiness is quenched.
10085-8{_}1669{_}And if I ask of him the cause,     He says the cause in me is found;   That I am vain, the rover I,     And to another's bosom bound.
10085-8{_}1670{_}As if, since I have known his love,     I at the window show my face,   Or take another's hand in mine,     Or seek the bull-ring, joust, or race;   Or if my footsteps have been found     To wander a suspected place,   The prophet's curse upon me fall,     Unless to keep the nuptial pact   And serve the pleasure of my lord.
10085-8{_}1671{_}I kept the Koran's law exact!
10085-8{_}1672{_}But wherefore should I waste the time     These tedious questions to recall?
10085-8{_}1673{_}Thou knowest the chase on which he hies,     And yet in silence hidest all.
10085-8{_}1674{_}Nay, swear not--I will naught believe;     Thine oaths are but a fowler's net,   And woe betide the dame who falls     Into the snare that thou hast set.
10085-8{_}1675{_}For men are traitors one and all;     And all their promises betray;   Like letters on the water writ,     They vanish, when love's fires decay.
10085-8{_}1676{_}For to fulfil thy promise fair,     What hours thou hast the whole day long,   What chances on the open road,     Or in the house when bolts are strong.
10085-8{_}1677{_}O God!
10085-8{_}1678{_}but what a thought is this?
10085-8{_}1679{_}I strangle, in the sudden thrall   Of this sharp pang of agony,     Oh, hold me, Tarfe, lest I fall."
10085-8{_}1680{_}Thus Adelifa weeping cried     At thought of Abenamar's quest:   In Moorish Tarfe's arms she fell,     And panting lay upon his breast.
10085-8{_}1681{_}THE CAPTIVE OF TOLEDO    Upon the loftiest mountain height     That rises in its pride,   And sees its summits mirrored     In Tagus' crystal tide,   The banished Abenamar,     Bound by a captive chain,   Looks on the high-road to Madrid     That seams the dusty plain.
10085-8{_}1682{_}He measures, with his pining eyes,     The stretching hills that stand   Between his place of banishment     And his sweet native land.
10085-8{_}1683{_}His sighs and tears of sorrow     No longer bear restraint,   And thus in words of anguish     He utters his complaint:   "Oh, dismal is the exile     That wrings the heart with woes   And locks the lips in silence,     Amid unfeeling foes.
10085-8{_}1684{_}O road of high adventure,     That leadest many a band   To yon ungrateful country where     My native turrets stand,   The country that my valor     Did oft with glory crown,   The land that lets me languish here,     Who won for her renown.
10085-8{_}1685{_}Thou who hast succored many a knight,     Hast thou no help for me,   Who languish on Toledo's height     In captive misery?
10085-8{_}1686{_}'Tis on thy world-wide chivalry     I base my word of blame,   'Tis that I love thee most of all,     Thy coldness brings me shame.
10085-8{_}1687{_}Oh, dismal is the exile,     That wrings my heart with woes,   And locks my lips in silence     Among unfeeling foes.
10085-8{_}1688{_}The warden of fierce Reduan     With cruelty more deep   That that of a hidalgo,     Has locked this prison keep;   And on this frontier set me,     To pine without repose,   To watch, from dawn to sunset,     Over his Christian foes.
10085-8{_}1689{_}Here like a watch-tower am I set     For Santiago's lord,   And for a royal mistress     Who breaks her plighted word.
10085-8{_}1690{_}And when I cry with anguish     And seek in song relief,   With threats my life is threatened,     Till silence cloak my grief.
10085-8{_}1691{_}Oh, dismal is the exile,     That wrings my heart with woes,   And locks my lips in silence     Among unfeeling foes.
10085-8{_}1692{_}And when I stand in silence,     Me dumb my jailers deem,   And if I speak, in gentle words,     They say that I blaspheme.
10085-8{_}1693{_}Thus grievously perverting     The sense of all I say,   Upon my lips the raging crowd     The gag of silence lay.
10085-8{_}1694{_}Thus heaping wrong on wrong my foes     Their prisoner impeach,   Until the outrage of my heart     Deprives my tongue of speech.
10085-8{_}1695{_}And while my word the passion     Of my sad heart betrays,   My foes are all unconscious     Of what my silence says.
10085-8{_}1696{_}Now God confound the evil judge     Who caused my misery,   And had no heart of pity     To soften his decree.
10085-8{_}1697{_}Oh, dismal is the exile,     That wrings my heart with woes,   And locks my lips in silence     Among unfeeling foes.
10085-8{_}1698{_}THE BLAZON OF ABENAMAR    By gloomy fortune overcast,     Vassal of one he held in scorn,   Complaining of the wintry world,     And by his lady left forlorn,   The wretched Abenamar mourned,     Because his country was unkind,   Had brought him to a lot of woe,     And to a foreign home resigned.
10085-8{_}1699{_}A stranger Moor had won the throne,     And in Granada sat in state.
10085-8{_}1700{_}Many the darlings of his soul     He claimed with love insatiate,   He, foul in face, of craven heart,     Had won the mistress of the knight;   Her blooming years of beauteous youth     Were Abenamar's own by right.
10085-8{_}1701{_}But royal favor had decreed     A foreign tyrant there should reign,   For many a galley owned him lord     And master, in the seas of Spain.
10085-8{_}1702{_}Oh, haply 'twas that Zaida's self,     Ungrateful like her changing sex,   Had chosen this emir, thus in scorn     Her Abenamar's soul to vex.
10085-8{_}1703{_}This was the thought that turned to tears     The eyes of the desponding knight,   As on his sufferings past he thought,     His labors and his present plight;   His hopes, to disappointment turned;     His wealth, now held in alien hands,   His agony o'er love betrayed,     Lost honor, confiscated lands.
10085-8{_}1704{_}And as his loyalty had met     Such ill requital from the King,   He called his page and bade him straight     A limner deft before him bring.
10085-8{_}1705{_}For he would have him paint at large,     In color, many a new device   And write his sufferings on his shield.
10085-8{_}1706{_}No single blazon would suffice.
10085-8{_}1707{_}And first a green field parched and seared;     A coal, in myriad blazes burned,   And like his ardent hopes of yore,     At length to dust and ashes turned.
10085-8{_}1708{_}And then a miser, rich in gold,     Who locks away some jewel bright,   For fear the thief a gem may steal,     Which yet can yield him no delight.
10085-8{_}1709{_}A fair Adonis done to death     Beneath the wild boar's cruel tusk.
10085-8{_}1710{_}A wintry dawn on pallid skies,     A summer's day that turns to dusk.
10085-8{_}1711{_}A lovely garden green and fair     Ravaged and slashed by strokes of steel;   Or wasted in its trim parterres     And trampled by the common heel.
10085-8{_}1712{_}So spake the brave heart-broken Moor;     Until his tears and struggling sighs   Turned to fierce rage; the painting then     He waited for with eager eyes.
10085-8{_}1713{_}He asks that one would fetch a steed,     Of his good mare no more he recks,   For womankind have done him wrong,     And she is woman in her sex.
10085-8{_}1714{_}The plumes of yellow, blue, and white     From off his bonnet brim he tears,   He will no longer carry them;     They are the colors Zaida wears.
10085-8{_}1715{_}He recks no more of woman's love,     His city now he bids farewell,   And swears he will no more return     Nor in Granada seek to dwell.
10085-8{_}1716{_}WOMAN'S FICKLENESS    A stout and valorous gentleman,     Granada knew his worth,   And rich with many a spoil of love,     Went Abenamar forth.
10085-8{_}1717{_}Upon his bonnet, richly dyed,     He bore a lettered scroll,   It ran, "'Tis only love that makes     The solace of my soul."
10085-8{_}1718{_}His bonnet and his brow were hid     Beneath a hood of green,   And plumes of violet and white     Above his head were seen.
10085-8{_}1719{_}And 'twixt the tassel and the crown     An emerald circlet shone.
10085-8{_}1720{_}The legend of the jewel said,     "Thou art my hope alone."
10085-8{_}1721{_}He rode upon a dappled steed     With housings richly dight,   And at his left side clanking hung     A scimitar of might.
10085-8{_}1722{_}And his right arm was sleeved in cloth     Of tawny lion's hue,   And at his lance-head, lifted high,     A Turkish pennon flew.
10085-8{_}1723{_}And when he reached Daraja's camp     He saw Daraja stand   Beside his own perfidious love,     And clasp her by the hand.
10085-8{_}1724{_}He made to her the wonted sign,     Then lingered for a while,   For jealous anguish filled his heart     To see her tender smile.
10085-8{_}1725{_}He spurred his courser to the blood;     One clattering bound he took,   The Moorish maiden turned to him.
10085-8{_}1726{_}Ah, love was in her look!
10085-8{_}1727{_}Ah, well he saw his hopeless fate,     And in his jealous mood   The heart that nothing feared in fight     Was whelmed in sorrow's flood.
10085-8{_}1728{_}"O false and faithless one," he said,     "What is it that I view?
10085-8{_}1729{_}Thus the foreboding of my soul     I see at last come true;   Shame that a janizary vile,     Of Christian creed and race,   A butt of bright Alhambra's feasts,     Has taken now my place.
10085-8{_}1730{_}Where is the love thou didst avow,     The pledge, the kiss, the tear,   And all the tender promises     Thou whisperedst in my ear?
10085-8{_}1731{_}Thou, frailer than the withered reed,     More changeful than the wind,   More thankless than the hardest heart     In all of womankind;   I marvel not at what I see,     Nor yet for vengeance call;   For thou art woman to the core,     And in that name is all."
10085-8{_}1732{_}The gallant Moor his courser checked,     His cheek with anger burned,   Men saw, that all his gallant mien     To gloom and rage was turned.
10085-8{_}1733{_}KING JUAN    "Abenamar, Abenamar," said the monarch to the knight,   "A Moor art thou of the Moors, I trow, and the ladies' fond delight,   And on the day when first you lay upon your mother's breast,   On land and sea was a prodigy, to the Christians brought unrest;   The sea was still as a ruined mill and the winds were hushed to rest.
10085-8{_}1734{_}And the broad, broad moon sank down at noon, red in the stormy west.
10085-8{_}1735{_}If thus thou wert born thou well mayst scorn to ope those lips of thine,   That out should fly a treacherous lie, to meet a word of mine."
10085-8{_}1736{_}"I have not lied," the Moor replied, and he bowed his haughty head   Before the King whose wrath might fling his life among the dead.
10085-8{_}1737{_}"I would not deign with falsehood's stain my lineage to betray;   Tho' for the truth my life, in sooth, should be the price I pay.
10085-8{_}1738{_}I am son and squire of a Moorish sire, who with the Christians strove,   And the captive dame of Christian name was his fair wedded love;   And I a child from that mother mild, who taught me at her knee   Was ever told to be true and bold with a tongue that was frank and free,   That the liar's art and the caitiff heart would lead to the house of                 doom;   And still I must hear my mother dear, for she speaks to me from the tomb.
10085-8{_}1739{_}Then give me my task, O King, and ask what question thou mayst choose;   I will give to you the word that is true, for why should I refuse?"
10085-8{_}1740{_}"I give you grace for your open face, and the courteous words you use.
10085-8{_}1741{_}What castles are those on the hill where grows the palm-tree and the                 pine?
10085-8{_}1742{_}They are so high that they touch the sky, and with gold their pinnacles                 shine."
10085-8{_}1743{_}"In the sunset's fire there glisten, sire, Alhambra's tinted tiles;   And somewhat lower Alijire's tower upon the vega smiles,   And many a band of subtile hand has wrought its pillared aisles.
10085-8{_}1744{_}The Moor whose thought and genius wrought those works for many moons   Received each day a princely pay--five hundred gold doubloons--   Each day he left his labor deft, his guerdon was denied;   Nor less he lost than his labor cost when he his hand applied.
10085-8{_}1745{_}And yonder I see the Generalifé with its orchard green and wide;   There are growing there the apple and pear that are Granada's pride.
10085-8{_}1746{_}There shadows fall from the soaring wall of high Bermeja's tower;   It has flourished long as a castle strong, the seat of the Soldan's                 power."
10085-8{_}1747{_}The King had bent and his ear had lent to the words the warrior spoke,   And at last he said, as he raised his head before the crowd of folk:   "I would take thee now with a faithful vow, Granada for my bride,   King Juan's Queen would hold, I ween, a throne and crown of pride;   That very hour I would give thee dower that well would suit thy will;   Cordova's town should be thine own, and the mosque of proud Seville.
10085-8{_}1748{_}Nay, ask not, King, for I wear the ring of a faithful wife and true;   Some graceful maid or a widow arrayed in her weeds is the wife for you,   And close I cling to the Moorish King who holds me to his breast,   For well I ween it can be seen that of all he loves me best."
10085-8{_}1749{_}ABENAMAR'S JEALOUSY    Alhambra's bell had not yet pealed   Its morning note o'er tower and field;   Barmeja's bastions glittered bright,   O'ersilvered with the morning light;   When rising from a pallet blest   With no refreshing dews of rest,   For slumber had relinquished there   His place to solitary care,   Brave Abenamar pondered deep   How lovers must surrender sleep.
10085-8{_}1750{_}And when he saw the morning rise,   While sleep still sealed Daraja's eyes,   Amid his tears, to soothe his pain,   He sang this melancholy strain:     "The morn is up,       The heavens alight,     My jealous soul       Still owns the sway of night.
10085-8{_}1751{_}Thro' all the night I wept forlorn,   Awaiting anxiously the morn;   And tho' no sunlight strikes on me,   My bosom burns with jealousy.
10085-8{_}1752{_}The twinkling starlets disappear;   Their radiance made my sorrow clear;   The sun has vanished from my sight,   Turned into water is his light;   What boots it that the glorious sun   From India his course has run,   To bring to Spain the gleam of day,   If from my sight he hides away?
10085-8{_}1753{_}The morn is up,       The heavens are bright,     My jealous soul       Still owns the sway of night."
10085-8{_}1754{_}ADELIFA'S JEALOUSY    Fair Adelifa sees in wrath, kindled by jealous flames,   Her Abenamar gazed upon by the kind Moorish dames.
10085-8{_}1755{_}And if they chance to speak to him, or take him by the hand,   She swoons to see her own beloved with other ladies stand.
10085-8{_}1756{_}When with companions of his own, the bravest of his race,   He meets the bull within the ring, and braves him to his face,   Or if he mount his horse of war, and sallying from his tent   Engages with his comrades in tilt or tournament,   She sits apart from all the rest, and when he wins the prize   She smiles in answer to his smile and devours him with her eyes.
10085-8{_}1757{_}And in the joyous festival and in Alhambra's halls,   She follows as he treads the dance at merry Moorish balls.
10085-8{_}1758{_}And when the tide of battle is rising o'er the land,   And he leaves his home, obedient to his honored King's command,   With tears and lamentation she sees the warrior go   With arms heroic to subdue the proud presumptuous foe.
10085-8{_}1759{_}Though 'tis to save his country's towers he mounts his fiery steed   She has no cheerful word for him, no blessing and godspeed;    And were there some light pretext to keep him at her side,   In chains of love she'd bind him there, whate'er the land betide.
10085-8{_}1760{_}Or, if 'twere fair that dames should dare the terrors of the fight,   She'd mount her jennet in his train and follow with delight.
10085-8{_}1761{_}For soon as o'er the mountain ridge his bright plume disappears,   She feels that in her heart the jealous smart that fills her eyes with                 tears.
10085-8{_}1762{_}Yet when he stands beside her and smiles beneath her gaze,   Her cheek is pale with passion pure, though few the words she says.
10085-8{_}1763{_}Her thoughts are ever with him, and they fly the mountain o'er   When in the shaggy forest he hunts the bristly boar.
10085-8{_}1764{_}In vain she seeks the festal scene 'mid dance and merry song,   Her heart for Abenamar has left that giddy throng.
10085-8{_}1765{_}For jealous passion after all is no ignoble fire,   It is the child of glowing love, the shadow of desire.
10085-8{_}1766{_}Ah!
10085-8{_}1767{_}he who loves with ardent breast and constant spirit must   Feel in his inmost bosom lodged the arrows of distrust.
10085-8{_}1768{_}And as the faithful lover by his loved one's empty seat   Knows that the wind of love may change e'er once again they meet,   So to this sad foreboding do fancied griefs appear   As he who has most cause to love has too most cause for fear.
10085-8{_}1769{_}And once, when placid evening was mellowing into night,   The lovely Adelifa sat with her darling knight;   And then the pent-up feeling from out her spirit's deeps   Rose with a storm of heavy sighs and trembled on her lips:   "My valiant knight, who art, indeed, the whole wide world to me,   Clear mirror of victorious arms and rose of chivalry,   Thou terror of thy valorous foe, to whom all champions yield,   The rampart and the castle of fair Granada's field,   In thee the armies of the land their bright example see,   And all their hopes of victory are founded upon thee;   And I, poor loving woman, have hope in thee no less,   For thou to me art life itself, a life of happiness.
10085-8{_}1770{_}Yet, in this anxious trembling heart strange pangs of fear arise,   Ah, wonder not if oft you see from out these faithful eyes   The tears in torrents o'er my cheek, e'en in thy presence flow.
10085-8{_}1771{_}Half prompted by my love for thee and half by fears of woe,   These eyes are like alembics, and when with tears they fill   It is the flame of passion that does that dew distil.
10085-8{_}1772{_}And what the source from which they flow, but the sorrow and the care   That gather in my heart like mist, and forever linger there.
10085-8{_}1773{_}And when the flame is fiercest and love is at its height,   The waters rise to these fond eyes, and rob me of my sight,   For love is but a lasting pain and ever goes with grief,   And only at the spring of tears the heart can drink relief.
10085-8{_}1774{_}Thus fire and love and fear combined bring to my heart distress,   With jealous rage and dark distrust alarm and fitfulness.
10085-8{_}1775{_}These rage within my bosom; they torment me till I'd weep.
10085-8{_}1776{_}By day and night without delight a lonely watch I keep.
10085-8{_}1777{_}By Allah, I beseech thee, if thou art true to me,   That when the Moorish ladies turn round and gaze on thee,   Thou wilt not glance again at them nor meet their smiling eye,   Or else, my Abenamar, I shall lay me down and die.
10085-8{_}1778{_}For thou art gallant, fair, and good; oh, soothe my heart's alarms,   And be as tender in thy love as thou art brave in arms.
10085-8{_}1779{_}And as they yield to thee the prize for valor in the field   Oh, show that thou wilt pity to thy loving lady yield."
10085-8{_}1780{_}Then Abenamar, with a smile, a kiss of passion gave.
10085-8{_}1781{_}"If it be needful," he replied, "to give the pledge you crave   To tell thee, Adelifa, that thou art my soul's delight   And lay my inmost bosom bare before thy anxious sight,   The bosom on whose mirror shines thy face in lines of light,   Here let me ope the secret cell that thou thyself may see,   The altar and the blazing lamp that always burn for thee.
10085-8{_}1782{_}And if perchance thou art not thus released from torturing care,   Oh, see the faith, the blameless love that wait upon thee there.
10085-8{_}1783{_}And if thou dost imagine I am a perjured knight,   I pray that Allah on my head may call down bane and blight,   And when into the battle with the Christian I go   I pray that I may perish by the lances of the foe;   And when I don my armor for the toils of the campaign,   That I may never wear the palm of victory again,   But as a captive, on a shore far from Granada, pine,   While the freedom that I long to have may never more be mine.
10085-8{_}1784{_}Yes, may my foes torment me in that sad hour of need;   My very friends, for their own ends, prove worthless as a reed.
10085-8{_}1785{_}My kin deny, my fortune fly, and, on my dying day,   My very hopes of Paradise in darkness pass away.
10085-8{_}1786{_}Or if I live in freedom to see my love once more,   May I meet the fate which most I hate, and at my palace door   Find that some caitiff lover has won thee for his own,   And turn to die, of mad despair, distracted and alone.
10085-8{_}1787{_}Wherefore, my life, my darling wife, let all thy pain be cured;   Thy trust in my fidelity be from this hour assured.
10085-8{_}1788{_}No more those pearly tears of thine fall useless in the dust   No more the jealous fear distract thy bosom with mistrust.
10085-8{_}1789{_}Believe me by the oath I swear my heart I here resign,   And all I have of love and care are, Adelifa, thine.
10085-8{_}1790{_}Believe that Abenamar would his own life betray   If he had courage thus to throw life's choicest gem away."
10085-8{_}1791{_}Then Adelifa smiled on him and at the words he said,   Upon his heaving bosom her blushing cheek she laid.
10085-8{_}1792{_}And from that hour each jealous thought far from her mind she thrust   And confidence returned again in place of dark distrust.
10085-8{_}1793{_}FUNERAL OF ABENAMAR    The Moors of haughty Gelves have changed their gay attire.
10085-8{_}1794{_}The caftan and the braided cloak, the brooch of twisted wire,   The gaudy robes, the mantles of texture rich and rare,   The fluttering veils and tunic bright the Moors no longer wear.
10085-8{_}1795{_}And wearied is their valorous strength, their sinewy arms hang down;   No longer in their lady's sight they struggle for the crown.
10085-8{_}1796{_}Whether their loves are absent or glowing in their eyes,   They think no more of jealous feud nor smile nor favor prize;   For love himself seems dead to-day amid that gallant train   And the dirge beside the bier is heard and each one joins the strain,   And silently they stand in line arrayed in mourning black   For the dismal pall of Portugal is hung on every back.
10085-8{_}1797{_}And their faces turned toward the bier where Abenamar lies,   The men his kinsmen silent stand, amid the ladies' cries   And thousand thousands ask and look upon the Moorish knight,   By his coat of steel they weeping kneel, then turn them from the sight.
10085-8{_}1798{_}And some proclaim his deeds of fame, his spirit high and brave,   And the courage of adventure that had brought him to the grave.
10085-8{_}1799{_}Some say that his heroic soul pined with a jealous smart,   That disappointment and neglect had broke that mighty heart;   That all his ancient hopes gave way beneath the cloud of grief,   Until his green and youthful years were withered like a leaf;   And he is wept by those he loved, by every faithful friend,   And those who slandered him in life speak evil to the end.
10085-8{_}1800{_}They found within his chamber where his arms of battle hung   A parting message written all in the Moorish tongue:   "Dear friends of mine, if ever in Gelves I should die,   I would not that in foreign soil my buried ashes lie.
10085-8{_}1801{_}But carry me, and dig my grave upon mine own estate,   And raise no monument to me my life to celebrate,   For banishment is not more dire where evil men abound,   Than where home smiles upon you, but the good are never found."
10085-8{_}1802{_}BALLAD OF ALBAYALDOS    Three mortal wounds, three currents red,     The Christian spear   Has oped in head and thigh and head--     Brave Albayaldos feels that death is near.
10085-8{_}1803{_}The master's hand had dealt the blow,     And long had been   And hard the fight; now in his heart's blood low     He wallows, and the pain, the pain is keen.
10085-8{_}1804{_}He raised to heaven his streaming face     And low he said:   "Sweet Jesus, grant me by thy grace,     Unharmed to make this passage to the dead.
10085-8{_}1805{_}"Oh, let me now my sins recount,     And grant at last   Into thy presence I may mount,     And thou, dear mother, think not of my past.
10085-8{_}1806{_}"Let not the fiend with fears affright     My trembling soul;   Though bitter, bitter is the night     Whose darkling clouds this moment round me roll.
10085-8{_}1807{_}"Had I but listened to your plea,     I ne'er had met   Disaster; though this life be lost to me,     Let not your ban upon my soul be set.
10085-8{_}1808{_}"In him, in him alone I trust,     To him I pray,   Who formed this wretched body from the dust.
10085-8{_}1809{_}He will redeem me in the Judgment Day.
10085-8{_}1810{_}"And Muza, one last service will I ask,     Dear friend of mine:   Here, where I died, be it thy pious task     To bury me beneath the tall green pine.
10085-8{_}1811{_}"And o'er my head a scroll indite, to tell     How, on this sod,   Fighting amid my valiant Moors, I fell.
10085-8{_}1812{_}And tell King Chico how I turned to God,    "And longed to be a Christian at the last,     And sought the light,   So that the accursed Koran could not cast     My soul to suffer in eternal night."
10085-8{_}1813{_}THE NIGHT RAID OF REDUAN    Two thousand are the Moorish knights that 'neath the banner stand   Of mighty Reduan, as he starts in ravage thro' the land.
10085-8{_}1814{_}With pillage and with fire he wastes the fields and fruitful farms,   And thro' the startled border-land is heard the call to arms;   By Jaen's towers his host advance and, like a lightning flash,   Ubeda and Andujar can see his horsemen dash,           While in Baeza every bell               Does the appalling tidings tell,               "Arm!
10085-8{_}1815{_}Arm!"
10085-8{_}1816{_}Rings on the night the loud alarm.
10085-8{_}1817{_}So silently they gallop, that gallant cavalcade,   The very trumpet's muffled tone has no disturbance made.
10085-8{_}1818{_}It seems to blend with the whispering sound of breezes on their way,   The rattle of their harness and the charger's joyous neigh.
10085-8{_}1819{_}But now from hill and turret high the flaming cressets stream   And watch-fires blaze on every hill and helm and hauberk gleam.
10085-8{_}1820{_}From post to post the signal along the border flies   And the tocsin sounds its summons and the startled burghers rise,           While in Baeza every bell               Does the appalling tidings tell,               "Arm!
10085-8{_}1821{_}Arm!"
10085-8{_}1822{_}Rings on the night the loud alarm.
10085-8{_}1823{_}Ah, suddenly that deadly foe has fallen upon the prey,   Yet stoutly rise the Christians and arm them for the foe,   And doughty knights their lances seize and scour their coats of mail,   The soldier with his cross-bow comes and the peasant with his flail.
10085-8{_}1824{_}And Jaen's proud hidalgos, Andujar's yeomen true,   And the lords of towered Ubeda the pagan foes pursue;   And valiantly they meet the foe nor turn their backs in flight,   And worthy do they show themselves of their fathers' deeds of might,           While in Baeza every bell               Does the appalling tidings tell,               "Arm!
10085-8{_}1825{_}Arm!"
10085-8{_}1826{_}Rings on the night the loud alarm.
10085-8{_}1827{_}The gates of dawn are opened and sunlight fills the land,   The Christians issuing from the gates in martial order stand,   They close in fight, and paynim host and Christian knights of Spain,   Not half a league from the city gate, are struggling on the plain.
10085-8{_}1828{_}The din of battle rises like thunder to the sky,   From many a crag and forest the thundering echoes fly,   And there is sound of clashing arms, of sword and rattling steel,   Moorish horns, the fife and drum, as the scattering squadrons reel,   And the dying moan and the wounded shriek for the hurt that none can                 heal,           While in Baeza every bell               Does the appalling tidings tell,               "Arm!
10085-8{_}1829{_}Arm!"
10085-8{_}1830{_}Rings on the night the loud alarm.
10085-8{_}1831{_}SIEGE OF JAEN    Now Reduan gazes from afar on Jaen's ramparts high,   And tho' he smiles in triumph yet fear is in his eye,   And vowed has he, whose courage none charged with a default,   That he would climb the ramparts and take it by assault,   Yet round the town the towers and walls the city's streets impale,   And who of all his squadrons that bastion can scale?
10085-8{_}1832{_}He pauses until one by one his hopes have died away,   And his soul is filled with anguish and his face with deep dismay.
10085-8{_}1833{_}He marks the tall escarpment, he measures with his eye   The soaring towers above them that seem to touch the sky.
10085-8{_}1834{_}Height upon height they mount to heaven, while glittering from afar   Each cresset on the watch-towers burns like to a baleful star.
10085-8{_}1835{_}His eyes and heart are fixed upon the rich and royal town,   And from his eye the tear of grief, a manly tear, flows down.
10085-8{_}1836{_}His bosom heaves with sighs of grief and heavy discontent,   As to the royal city he makes his sad lament:   "Ah, many a champion have I lost, fair Jaen, at thy gate,   Yet lightly did I speak of thee with victory elate,   The prowess of my tongue was more than all that I could do,   And my word outstripped the lance and sword of my squadron strong and                 true.
10085-8{_}1837{_}And yet I vowed with courage rash thy turrets I would bring   To ruin and thy subjects make the captives of my King.
10085-8{_}1838{_}That in one night my sword of might, before the morrow's sun,   Would do for thy great citadel what centuries have not done.
10085-8{_}1839{_}I pledged my life to that attempt, and vowed that thou shouldest fall,   Yet now I stand in impotence before thy castle tall.
10085-8{_}1840{_}For well I see, before my might shall win thee for my King,   That thou, impregnable, on me wilt rout and ruin bring,   Ah, fatal is the hasty tongue that gives such quick consent,   And he who makes the hasty vow in leisure must repent.
10085-8{_}1841{_}Ah!
10085-8{_}1842{_}now too late I mourn the word that sent me on this quest,   For I see that death awaits me here whilst thou livest on at rest,   For I must enter Jaen's gates a conqueror or be sent   Far from Granada's happy hills in hopeless banishment;   But sorest is the thought that I to Lindaraja swore:   If Jaen should repulse me I'd return to her no more;   No more a happy lover would I linger at her side,   Until Granada's warrior host had humbled Jaen's pride."
10085-8{_}1843{_}Then turning to his warriors, the Moorish cavalier   Asks for their counsel and awaits their answer while with fear.
10085-8{_}1844{_}Five thousand warriors tried and true the Moors were standing near,   All armed with leathern buckler, all armed with sword and spear.
10085-8{_}1845{_}"The place," they answer, "is too strong, by walls too high 'tis bound,   Too many are the watch-towers that circle it around.
10085-8{_}1846{_}The knights and proud hidalgos who on the wall are seen,   Their hearts are bold, their arms are strong, their swords and spears are                 keen.
10085-8{_}1847{_}Disaster will be certain as the rising of the day,   And victory and booty are a slippery prize," they say,   "It would be wise in this emprise the conflict to forego;   Not all the Moors Granada boasts could lay proud Jaen low."
10085-8{_}1848{_}THE DEATH OF REDUAN    He shrank not from his promise, did Reduan the brave,   The promise to Granada's King with daring high he gave;   And when the morning rose and lit the hills with ruddy glow,   He marshalled forth his warriors to strike a final blow.
10085-8{_}1849{_}With shouts they hurry to the walls, ten thousand fighting men--   Resolved to plant the crescent on the bulwarks of Jaen.
10085-8{_}1850{_}The bugle blast upon the air with clarion tone is heard,   The burghers on the city wall reply with scoffing word;   And like the noise of thunder the clattering squadrons haste,   And on his charger fleet he leads his army o'er the waste.
10085-8{_}1851{_}In front of his attendants his march the hero made,   He tarried not for retinue or clattering cavalcade,   And they who blamed the rash assault with weak and coward minds   Deserted him their leader bold or loitered far behind.
10085-8{_}1852{_}And now he stands beneath the wall and sees before him rise   The object of the great campaign, his valor's priceless prize;   He dreams one moment that he holds her subject to his arms,   He dreams that to Granada he flies from war's alarms,   Each battlement he fondly eyes, each bastion grim and tall,   And in fancy sees the crescents rise above the Christian wall.
10085-8{_}1853{_}But suddenly an archer has drawn his bow of might,   And suddenly the bolt descends in its unerring flight,   Straight to the heart of Reduan the fatal arrow flies,   The gallant hero struck to death upon the vega lies.
10085-8{_}1854{_}And as he lies, from his couch of blood, in melancholy tone,   Thus to the heavens the hero stout, though fainting, makes his moan,   And ere his lofty soul in death forth from its prison breaks,   Brave Reduan a last farewell of Lindaraja takes:   "Ah, greater were the glory had it been mine to die,   Not thus among the Christians and hear their joyful cry,   But in that happy city, reclining at thy feet,   Where thou with kind and tender hands hast wove my winding-sheet.
10085-8{_}1855{_}Ah!
10085-8{_}1856{_}had it been my fate once more to gaze upon thy face,   And love and pity in those eyes with dying glance to trace,   Altho' a thousand times had death dissolved this mortal frame,   Soon as thy form before me in radiant beauty came,   A thousand times one look of thine had given me back my breath,   And called thy lover to thy side even from the gate of death.
10085-8{_}1857{_}What boots it, Lindaraja, that I, at Jaen's gate,   That unsurrendered city, have met my final fate?
10085-8{_}1858{_}What boots it, that this city proud will ne'er the Soldan own,   For thee and not for Jaen this hour I make my moan;   I weep for Lindaraja, I weep to think that she   May mourn a hostage and a slave in long captivity.
10085-8{_}1859{_}But worse than this that some proud Moor will take thee to his heart,   And all thy thoughts of Reduan new love may bid depart.
10085-8{_}1860{_}And dwelling on thy beauty he will deem it better far,   To win fair Lindaraja than all the spoils of war,   Yet would I pray if Mahomet, whose servant I have been,   Should ever from the throne of God look on this bloody scene,   And deem it right to all my vows requital fit to make,   And for my valor who attacked the town I could not take,   That he would make thy constancy as steadfast as the tower   Of Jaen's mighty fortress, that withstood the Moorish power;   Now as my life be ebbing fast, my spirit is oppressed,   And Reduan the warrior bold is sinking to his rest,   Oh, may my prayers be answered, if so kind heaven allow,   And may the King forgive me for the failure of my vow,   And, Lindaraja, may my soul, when it has taken its flight,   And for the sweet Elysian fields exchange these realms of night,   Contented in the joys and peace of that celestial seat,   Await the happy moment when we once more shall meet."
10085-8{_}1861{_}THE AGED LOVER    'Twas from a lofty balcony Arselia looked down   On golden Tagus' crystal stream that hemmed Toledo's town;   And now she watched the eddies that dimpled in the flood   And now she landward turned her eye to gaze on waste and wood,   But in all that lay around her she sought for rest in vain,   For her heart, her heart was aching, and she could not heal the pain.
10085-8{_}1862{_}'Tis of no courtly gallant the Moorish damsel dreams,   No lordly emir who commands the fort by Tagus' streams,   'Twas on the banks of Tornes stood the haughty towers of note   Where the young alcaydé loved by the maid from cities dwelt remote.
10085-8{_}1863{_}And never at Almanzor's court had he for honor sought,   Though he dwelt in high Toledo in fair Arselia's thought;   And now she dreams of love's great gift, of passion's deep delight,   When far away from her palace walls a stranger came in sight.
10085-8{_}1864{_}It was no gallant lovelorn youth she saw approaching fast,   It was the hero Reduan whose vernal years were past.
10085-8{_}1865{_}He rode upon a sorrel horse and swiftly he came nigh,   And stood where the dazzling sun beat down upon her balcony;   And with a thoughtful air upon the maiden turned his eye,   For suddenly the aged knight feels all his heart on fire,   And all the frost of his broken frame is kindling with desire.
10085-8{_}1866{_}And while he fain would hide his pain he paces up and down   Before the palace turrets that Toledo's rampart crown.
10085-8{_}1867{_}With anger glows the maiden's mind, "Now get thee gone," she cries,   "For can it be that love of me in blood like thine can rise?
10085-8{_}1868{_}I sicken at the very thought; thy locks, old man, are gray,   Thy baldness and thy trembling hand a doting age betray.
10085-8{_}1869{_}Ah, little must thou count my years of beauty and of bloom,   If thou wouldst wed them with a life thus tottering to the tomb,   Decrepitude is now thy lot, and wherefore canst thou dare   To ask that youthful charms these vile infirmities should share?"
10085-8{_}1870{_}And Moorish Reduan heard her words, and saw the meaning plain.
10085-8{_}1871{_}Advancing to the balcony he answered her again:   "The sun is king of everything, o'er all he holds his sway,   And thou art like the sun--thy charms I own and I obey;   Thy beauty warms my veins again, and in its rays, forsooth,   I feel the blithe, courageous mood of long-forgotten youth;   Sure love of mine can harm thee not, as sunlight is not lost   When its kind radiance dissolves the fetters of the frost."
10085-8{_}1872{_}Then turning round, a parchment did Reduan unfold,   And on it was a writing in characters of gold;   The meaning of the posy at once the maiden caught:   "Since I can venture, I can have; as yet, I am not naught."
10085-8{_}1873{_}He shows upon his shield a sun, circled with burning rays;   And on the rim was written a little verse which says,   "Two suns, one on my shield, and one in beauty's eyes, I trace."
10085-8{_}1874{_}Then at the cold disdain he saw upon her lovely face,   He covered with a gauzy veil the blazon of his shield,   "The sun upon my targe," he cried, "before thy light must yield."
10085-8{_}1875{_}But as the maid still pouted and eyed him with disdain,   "The mimic sun," continued he, "which here is blazoned plain,   Is overcast and hides itself from the true orb of day,   And I by beauty's radiance eclipsed must ride away."
10085-8{_}1876{_}And as he spoke the Moor struck deep the rowels in his steed,   And rode away from Tagus' side across the grassy mead.
10085-8{_}1877{_}The Moorish maiden recked not if he were far or near,   Her thoughts returned to fancies sweet of her absent cavalier.
10085-8{_}1878{_}FICKLENESS REBUKED    While in the foeman's ruddy gore     I waded to the breast,   And for mine own, my native shore     Fought braver than the best,   While the light cloak I laid aside,     And doffed the damask fold,   And donned my shirt of mail, the spoil     Of foeman brave and bold,   Thou, fickle Mooress, puttest on     Thine odorous brocade,   And hand in hand with thy false love     Wert sitting in the shade.
10085-8{_}1879{_}Thus on the scutcheon of thy sires     Thou plantest many a stain;   The pillars of thine ancient house     Will ne'er be firm again.
10085-8{_}1880{_}But, oh, may Allah vengeance take     For thine unkind deceit,   And sorely weeping mayst thou pay     The vengeance that is meet.
10085-8{_}1881{_}Thus shalt thou pay--thy lover's bliss     Thou shalt not, canst not share,   But feel the bitter mockery     Thy day-long shame must bear.
10085-8{_}1882{_}And what revenge 'twill be to note     When thou dost kiss his brow,   How thy gold tresses, soft and light,     Blend with his locks of snow;   And what revenge to hear him     To thee his loves recount,   Praising some Moorish lass, or mark     His sons thy staircase mount.
10085-8{_}1883{_}Yes, thou shalt pay the penalty,     When, from sweet Genil's side,   Thou passest to the stormy waves     Of Tagus' rushing tide;   Abencerrajes are not there,     And from thy balcony   Thou shalt not hear the horsemen     With loud hoof rushing by.
10085-8{_}1884{_}Thoughts of lost days shall haunt thee then     And lay thy spirit waste,   When thy past glories thou shalt see     All faded and effaced;   All gone, those sweet, seductive wiles--     The love note's scented scroll--   The words, and blushing vows, that brought     Damnation to thy soul.
10085-8{_}1885{_}Thus the bright moments of the past     Shall rise to memory's eye,   Like vengeance-bearing ministers     To mock thy misery.
10085-8{_}1886{_}For time is father of distress;     And he whose life is long   Experiences a thousand cares,     A thousand shapes of wrong.
10085-8{_}1887{_}Thou shalt be hated in the court,     And hated in the stall,   Hated in merry gathering,     In dance and festival.
10085-8{_}1888{_}Thou shalt be hated far and wide;     And, thinking on this hate,   Wilt lay it to the black offence     That thou didst perpetrate.
10085-8{_}1889{_}Then thou wilt make some weak defence,     And plead a father's will,   That forced thee shuddering to consent     To do the act of ill.
10085-8{_}1890{_}Enjoy then him whom thus constrained     Thou choosest for thine own;   But know, when love would have his way,     He scorns a father's frown.
10085-8{_}1891{_}THE GALLEY-SLAVE OF DRAGUT    Ah, fortune's targe and butt was he,     On whom were rained the strokes from hate   From love that had not found its goal,     From strange vicissitudes of fate.
10085-8{_}1892{_}A galley-slave of Dragut he,     Who once had pulled the laboring oar,   Now, 'mid a garden's leafy boughs,     He worked and wept in anguish sore.
10085-8{_}1893{_}"O Mother Spain!
10085-8{_}1894{_}for thy blest shore     Mine eyes impatient yearn;   For thy choicest gem is bride of mine,     And she longs for my return.
10085-8{_}1895{_}They took me from the galley bench;     A gardener's slave they set me here,   That I might tend the fruit and flowers     Through all the changes of the year;   Wise choice, indeed, they made of me!
10085-8{_}1896{_}For when the drought has parched the field,   The clouds that overcast my heart     Shall rain in every season yield.
10085-8{_}1897{_}O mother Spain!
10085-8{_}1898{_}for thy blest shore     Mine eyes impatient yearn;   For thy choicest gem is bride of mine,     And she longs for my return.
10085-8{_}1899{_}"They took me from the galley's hold;     It was by heaven's all-pitying grace.
10085-8{_}1900{_}Yet, even in this garden glade,     Has fortune turned away her face.
10085-8{_}1901{_}Though lighter now my lot of toil,     Yet is it heavier, since no more   My tear-dimmed eyes, my heart discern,     Across the sea, my native shore.
10085-8{_}1902{_}O mother Spain!
10085-8{_}1903{_}for thy blest shore     Mine eyes impatient yearn;   For thy choicest gem is bride of mine,     And she longs for my return.
10085-8{_}1904{_}"And you, ye exiles, who afar     In many a foreign land have strayed;   And from strange cities o'er the sea     A second fatherland have made--   Degenerate sons of glorious Spain!
10085-8{_}1905{_}One thing ye lacked to keep you true,   The love no stranger land could share;     The courage that could fate subdue.
10085-8{_}1906{_}O mother Spain!
10085-8{_}1907{_}for thy blest shore     Mine eyes impatient yearn;   For thy choicest gem is bride of mine,     And she longs for my return."
10085-8{_}1908{_}THE CAPTIVE'S LAMENT    Where Andalusia's plains at length end in the rocky shore,   And the billows of the Spanish sea against her boundaries roar,   A thousand ruined castles, that were once the haughty pride   Of high Cadiz, in days long past, looked down upon the tide.
10085-8{_}1909{_}And on the loftiest of them all, in melancholy mood,   A solitary captive that stormy evening stood.
10085-8{_}1910{_}For he had left the battered skiff that near the land wash lay,   And here he sought to rest his soul, and while his grief away,       While now, like furies, from the east the gale began to blow,       And with the crash of thunder the billows broke below.
10085-8{_}1911{_}Ah, yes, beneath the fierce levant, the wild white horses pranced;   With rising rage the billows against those walls advanced;   But stormier were the thoughts that filled his heart with bitter pain,   As he turned his tearful eyes once more to gaze upon the main.
10085-8{_}1912{_}"O hostile sea," these words at last burst from his heaving breast;   "I know that I return to die, but death at least is rest.
10085-8{_}1913{_}Then let me on my native shore again in freedom roam,   For here alone is shelter, for here at last is home."
10085-8{_}1914{_}And now, like furies, from the east the gale began to blow,       And with the crash of thunder the billows broke below.
10085-8{_}1915{_}'Twas Tagus' banks to me a child my home and nurture gave;   Ungrateful land, that lets me pine unransomed as a slave.
10085-8{_}1916{_}For now to-day, a dying man, am I come back again,   And I must lay my bones on this, the farthest shore of Spain.
10085-8{_}1917{_}It is not only exile's sword that cuts me to the heart;   It is not only love for her from whom they bade me part;   Nor only that I suffer, forgot by every friend,   But, ah!
10085-8{_}1918{_}it is the triple blow that brings me to my end."
10085-8{_}1919{_}And now, like furies, from the east the gale began to blow,       And with the crash of thunder the billows broke below.
10085-8{_}1920{_}"The fire with which my bosom burns, alas!
10085-8{_}1921{_}thy coolest breeze   Can never slake, nor can its rage thy coolest wave appease;   The earth can bring no solace to the ardor of my pain,   And the whole ocean waters were poured on it in vain.
10085-8{_}1922{_}For it is like the blazing sun that sinks in ocean's bed,   And yet, with ardor all unquenched, next morning rears its head.
10085-8{_}1923{_}Thus from the sea my suffering's flame has driven me once more,   And here I land, without a hope, upon this arid shore."
10085-8{_}1924{_}And now, like furies, from the east the gale began to blow,         And with the crash of thunder the billows broke below.
10085-8{_}1925{_}"Oh, call me not, oh, call me not, thou voice of other years,   The fire that flames within my heart has dried the spring of tears.
10085-8{_}1926{_}And, while my eyes might well pour forth those bitter drops of pain,   The drought of self-consuming grief has quenched the healing rain.
10085-8{_}1927{_}Here, let me cry aloud for her, whom once I called mine own,   For well I wot that loving maid for me has made her moan.
10085-8{_}1928{_}'Tis for her sake my flight I urge across the sea and land,   And now 'twixt shore and ocean's roar I take my final stand."
10085-8{_}1929{_}And now, like furies, from the east the gale began to blow,         And with the crash of thunder the billows broke below.
10085-8{_}1930{_}Then stooping to the earth he grasped the soil with eager hand,   He kissed it, and with water he mixed the thirsty sand.
10085-8{_}1931{_}"O thou," he said, "poor soil and stream, in the Creator's plan   Art the end and the beginning of all that makes us man!
10085-8{_}1932{_}From thee rise myriad passions, that stir the human breast,   To thee at last, when all is o'er, they sink to find their rest.
10085-8{_}1933{_}Thou, Earth, hast been my mother, and when these pangs are o'er,   Thou shalt become my prison-house whence I can pass no more."
10085-8{_}1934{_}And now, like furies, from the east the gale began to blow,       And with the crash of thunder the billows broke below.
10085-8{_}1935{_}And now he saw the warring winds that swept across the bay   Had struck the battered shallop and carried it away.
10085-8{_}1936{_}"O piteous heaven," he cried aloud, "my hopes are like yon bark:   Scattered upon the storm they lie and never reach their mark."
10085-8{_}1937{_}And suddenly from cloudy heavens came down the darkling night   And in his melancholy mood the captive left the height.
10085-8{_}1938{_}He gained his boat, with trembling hand he seized the laboring oar   And turning to the foaming wave he left his native shore.
10085-8{_}1939{_}"Ah, well I wot on ocean's breast when loud the tempest blows   Will rest be found when solid ground denies the heart repose.
10085-8{_}1940{_}Now let the hostile sea perceive no power of hers I dread,   But rather ask her vengeance may fall upon my head."
10085-8{_}1941{_}Into the night the shallop turned, while floated far behind   The captive's lamentation like a streamer on the wind.
10085-8{_}1942{_}And now, like furies, from the east the gale began to blow,       And with the crash of thunder the billows broke below.
10085-8{_}1943{_}STRIKE SAIL!
10085-8{_}1944{_}A Turkish bark was on the sea, the sunny sea of Spain,   In sight of cliffs that Hercules made boundaries of the main;   And one, Celimo's captive slave, as fierce the billows grew,   Was listening as the ship-master this order gave the crew:           "Strike sail!
10085-8{_}1945{_}Strike sail!
10085-8{_}1946{_}The furious gale           Is rising fast!
10085-8{_}1947{_}Strike sail!"
10085-8{_}1948{_}Fierce fell on them the opposing winds, the ship was helpless driven;   And with the ocean's flood were blent the thunder-drops of heaven.
10085-8{_}1949{_}And as the inky clouds were rent, the fiery lightning flared,   And 'mid the terror-stricken crew one voice alone was heard:         "Strike sail!
10085-8{_}1950{_}Strike sail!
10085-8{_}1951{_}The furious gale         Is rising fast!
10085-8{_}1952{_}Strike sail!"
10085-8{_}1953{_}And one there sat upon the deck, in captive misery,   Whose tears ran mingling with the flood, the flood of sky and sea.
10085-8{_}1954{_}Lost in the tempest of his thoughts, he fondly breathed a prayer,   Whose mournful words were echoed by the mount of his despair:         "Strike sail!
10085-8{_}1955{_}Strike sail!
10085-8{_}1956{_}The furious gale         Is rising fast!
10085-8{_}1957{_}Strike sail!"
10085-8{_}1958{_}"If I am captive and a slave, the time shall come when God   Will bring me freed, to tread once more my own, my native sod!
10085-8{_}1959{_}Then all my ancient glory shall return to me for aye.
10085-8{_}1960{_}Till then, my soul, be patient and wait that happy day!"
10085-8{_}1961{_}"Strike sail!
10085-8{_}1962{_}Strike sail!
10085-8{_}1963{_}The furious gale         Is rising fast!
10085-8{_}1964{_}Strike sail!"
10085-8{_}1965{_}THE CAPTIVE'S ESCAPE    The fair Florida sat at ease, upon a summer's day,   Within a garden green and fair that by the river lay,   And gayly asked that he her spouse would tell his darling wife   The cause of his captivity, the history of his life.
10085-8{_}1966{_}"Now tell me, dearest husband, I pray thee tell me true,   Who were thy parents, and what land thy birth and nurture knew?
10085-8{_}1967{_}And wherefore did they take thee a captive from that place,   And who has given thee liberty, thy homeward path to trace?"
10085-8{_}1968{_}"Yes, I will tell thee, gentle wife, and I will tell thee true,   For tender is the light I see within thine eyes of blue.
10085-8{_}1969{_}In Ronda did my father raise his castle on the height;   And 'twas in Antequera first my mother saw the light.
10085-8{_}1970{_}Me, to this dark captivity, the dastard Moors ensnared,   Just as the peace had ended and war was not declared.
10085-8{_}1971{_}They took me off in fetters, to barter me for gold,   Velez-de-la-Gomèra was the town where I was sold.
10085-8{_}1972{_}Seven weary days, and for each day a long and weary night,   They set me on the auction-block, before the people's sight.
10085-8{_}1973{_}Yet not a Moorish gentleman and not a Moorish wife   A maravedi offered for the mournful captive's life.
10085-8{_}1974{_}At last there came a Moorish dog, in rich attire, and gave   A thousand golden pieces to have me for his slave.
10085-8{_}1975{_}He led me to his lofty house, and bade me there remain,   Mocked by his lowest underlings, and loaded with a chain.
10085-8{_}1976{_}Ah!
10085-8{_}1977{_}vile the life he led me, and deep revenge I swore;   Ah!
10085-8{_}1978{_}black the life he gave me, and hard the toils I bore!
10085-8{_}1979{_}By day I beat the piled-up hemp cut from the vega plain;   By night, within the darkened mill, I ground for him the grain.
10085-8{_}1980{_}And though the very corn I ground, I longed to take for meat,   He placed a bridle on my mouth that I should nothing eat!
10085-8{_}1981{_}Therefore, it pleased the God who rules the heavens, the land, the sea,   That the mistress of that mighty house looked tenderly on me.
10085-8{_}1982{_}And when the Moor a-hunting went, one happy autumn day,   She came into my prison-house and took my chains away;   She bade me sit upon her lap, I answered with delight;   Ah, many a gallant present she made to me that night!
10085-8{_}1983{_}She bathed me and she washed my wounds, and garments fresh she gave,   Far brighter than were fit to deck the body of a slave;   And love's delight we shared that night, for I grew gay and bold!
10085-8{_}1984{_}And in the morn she gave to me a hundred crowns of gold.
10085-8{_}1985{_}She oped the gates, she bade me, with smiles, once more be free;   We fled, for fear that Moorish hound would slay both her and me.
10085-8{_}1986{_}And so it pleased the God who rules the earth and heavens above,   To prove his deep compassion and the greatness of his love;   And thus my sad captivity, my days of wandering, o'er,   Florida, in thy loving arms I nestle as of yore!"
10085-8{_}1987{_}THE SPANIARD OF ORAN    Right gallant was that gentleman, the warlike knight of Spain,   Who served the King in Oran, with sword and lances twain;   But, with his heart's devotion and passion's ardent fire,   He served a gentle Afric maid of high and noble sire.
10085-8{_}1988{_}And she was fair as noble, and well could she requite   The devotion of a lover and the courage of a knight.
10085-8{_}1989{_}And when one summer evening they paid their vows again,   They heard the alarum ring to arms across the darkling plain;   For the foes' approach had roused the watch and caused the war-like                 sound.
10085-8{_}1990{_}The silver moon had shed its ray upon their targes round,   The targes shot the message to the silent watch-towers by,   And watch-towers sent their tidings by flames that lit the sky;   And the fires had called the bells on high to ring their clear alarms--   That tocsin roused the lover locked in the lady's arms.
10085-8{_}1991{_}Ah, sorely felt he in his heart the spur of honor prick,   But love's appeal that held him, it pierced him to the quick.
10085-8{_}1992{_}'Twas cowardice to dally and shrink that foe to face,   But, ah, it was ingratitude to leave her in that case.
10085-8{_}1993{_}And hanging round her lover's neck, she saw that he turned pale,   And seized his sword and cast one glance upon his coat of mail;   And, with a burst of sighs and tears she bowed her beauteous head;   "Oh, rise, my lord, gird on thy arms, and join the fray," she said;   "Oh, let my tears this couch bedew; this couch of joy shall be   As dolorous as the dreary field of battle, without thee!
10085-8{_}1994{_}Arm, arm thyself and go to war!
10085-8{_}1995{_}Hark, hark!
10085-8{_}1996{_}the foes approach.
10085-8{_}1997{_}Thy general waits; oh, let him not thy knightliness reproach!
10085-8{_}1998{_}Oh, direly will he visit thee for cowardice to-day,   For dire the crime in any clime of soldiers who betray.
10085-8{_}1999{_}Well canst thou glide unnoticed to the camp, without thy sword;   Wilt thou not heed my tears, my sighs--begone without a word!
10085-8{_}2000{_}Thy bosom is not made of flesh, for, ah!
10085-8{_}2001{_}thou canst not feel,   Thou hast no need of arms in fight, for it is hard as steel."
10085-8{_}2002{_}The Spaniard gazed upon her, his heart was full of pride;   She held him fast and even her words retained him at her side.
10085-8{_}2003{_}"Lady," he said, and kissed her, "spite of thy words unwise,   Thou art as sweet as ever in thy lover's faithful eyes.
10085-8{_}2004{_}And since to love and honor this night thou hast appealed,   I take my arms and go, for right it is to thee I yield;   I go into the battle and my body seeks the fight,   But my soul behind me lingers in thy bosom of delight;   Oh, grant me, Lord and Master, to seek the camp below,   Oh, let me take the name to-night and I will cheerful go,   Bearing the sword, the lance, and coat of mail against the foe!"
10085-8{_}2005{_}MOORISH ROMANCES  [_Metrical Translation by J. Lockhart_]     MOORISH ROMANCES   THE BULL-FIGHT OF GAZUL  [Gazul is the name of one of the Moorish heroes who figure in the "_Historia de las Guerras Civiles de Granada_."
10085-8{_}2006{_}The following ballad is one of very many in which the dexterity of the Moorish cavaliers in the bull-fight is described.
10085-8{_}2007{_}The reader will observe that the shape, activity, and resolution of the unhappy animal destined to furnish the amusement of the spectators, are enlarged upon, just as the qualities of a modern race-horse might be among ourselves: nor is the bull without his name.
10085-8{_}2008{_}The day of the Baptist is a festival among the Mussulmans, as well as among Christians.]
10085-8{_}2009{_}King Almanzor of Granada, he hath bid the trumpet sound,   He hath summonded all the Moorish lords, from the hills and plains                 around;   From vega and sierra, from Betis and Xenil,   They have come with helm and cuirass of gold and twisted steel.
10085-8{_}2010{_}Tis the holy Baptist's feast they hold in royalty and state,   And they have closed the spacious lists beside the Alhambra's gate;   In gowns of black and silver laced, within the tented ring,   Eight Moors to fight the bull are placed in presence of the King.
10085-8{_}2011{_}Eight Moorish lords of valor tried, with stalwart arm and true,   The onset of the beasts abide, as they come rushing through;   The deeds they've done, the spoils they've won, fill all with hope and                 trust,   Yet ere high in heaven appears the sun they all have bit the dust.
10085-8{_}2012{_}Then sounds the trumpet clearly, then clangs the loud tambour,   Make room, make room for Gazul--throw wide, throw wide the door;   Blow, blow the trumpet clearer still, more loudly strike the drum,   The Alcaydé of Algava to fight the bull doth come.
10085-8{_}2013{_}And first before the King he passed, with reverence stooping low,   And next he bowed him to the Queen, and the Infantas all a-row;   Then to his lady's grace he turned, and she to him did throw   A scarf from out her balcony was whiter than the snow.
