10085-8{_}2014{_}With the life-blood of the slaughtered lords all slippery is the sand,   Yet proudly in the centre hath Gazul ta'en his stand;   And ladies look with heaving breast, and lords with anxious eye,   But firmly he extends his arm--his look is calm and high.
10085-8{_}2015{_}Three bulls against the knight are loosed, and two come roaring on,   He rises high in stirrup, forth stretching his rejón;   Each furious beast upon the breast he deals him such a blow   He blindly totters and gives back, across the sand to go.
10085-8{_}2016{_}"Turn, Gazul, turn," the people cry--the third comes up behind,   Low to the sand his head holds he, his nostrils snuff the wind;   The mountaineers that lead the steers, without stand whispering low,   "Now thinks this proud alcaydé to stun Harpado so?"
10085-8{_}2017{_}From Guadiana comes he not, he comes not from Xenil,   From Gaudalarif of the plain, or Barves of the hill;   But where from out the forest burst Xarama's waters clear,   Beneath the oak-trees was he nursed, this proud and stately steer.
10085-8{_}2018{_}Dark is his hide on either side, but the blood within doth boil,   And the dun hide glows, as if on fire, as he paws to the turmoil.
10085-8{_}2019{_}His eyes are jet, and they are set in crystal rings of snow;   But now they stare with one red glare of brass upon the foe.
10085-8{_}2020{_}Upon the forehead of the bull the horns stand close and near,   From out the broad and wrinkled skull, like daggers they appear;   His neck is massy, like the trunk of some old knotted tree,   Whereon the monster's shaggy mane, like billows curled, ye see.
10085-8{_}2021{_}His legs are short, his hams are thick, his hoofs are black as night,   Like a strong flail he holds his tail in fierceness of his might;   Like something molten out of iron, or hewn from forth the rock,   Harpado of Xarama stands, to bide the alcaydé's shock.
10085-8{_}2022{_}Now stops the drum--close, close they come--thrice meet, and thrice give                 back;   The white foam of Harpado lies on the charger's breast of black--   The white foam of the charger on Harpado's front of dun--   Once more advance upon his lance--once more, thou fearless one!
10085-8{_}2023{_}Once more, once more;--in dust and gore to ruin must thou reel--   In vain, in vain thou tearest the sand with furious heel--   In vain, in vain, thou noble beast, I see, I see thee stagger,   Now keen and cold thy neck must hold the stern alcaydé's dagger!
10085-8{_}2024{_}They have slipped a noose around his feet, six horses are brought in,   And away they drag Harpado with a loud and joyful din.
10085-8{_}2025{_}Now stoop thee, lady, from thy stand, and the ring of price bestow   Upon Gazul of Algava, that hath laid Harpado low.
10085-8{_}2026{_}THE ZEGRI'S BRIDE  [The reader cannot need to be reminded of the fatal effects which were produced by the feuds subsisting between the two great families, or rather races, of the Zegris and the Abencerrages of Granada.
10085-8{_}2027{_}The following ballad is also from the "_Guerras Civiles_."]
10085-8{_}2028{_}Of all the blood of Zegri, the chief is Lisaro,   To wield rejón like him is none, or javelin to throw;   From the place of his dominion, he ere the dawn doth go,   From Alcala de Henares, he rides in weed of woe.
10085-8{_}2029{_}He rides not now as he was wont, when ye have seen him speed   To the field of gay Toledo, to fling his lusty reed;   No gambeson of silk is on, nor rich embroidery   Of gold-wrought robe or turban--nor jewelled tahali.
10085-8{_}2030{_}No amethyst nor garnet is shining on his brow,   No crimson sleeve, which damsels weave at Tunis, decks him now;   The belt is black, the hilt is dim, but the sheathed blade is bright;   They have housened his barb in a murky garb, but yet her hoofs are light.
10085-8{_}2031{_}Four horsemen good, of the Zegri blood, with Lisaro go out;   No flashing spear may tell them near, but yet their shafts are stout;   In darkness and in swiftness rides every armed knight--   The foam on the rein ye may see it plain, but nothing else is white.
10085-8{_}2032{_}Young Lisaro, as on they go, his bonnet doffeth he,   Between its folds a sprig it holds of a dark and glossy tree;   That sprig of bay, were it away, right heavy heart had he--   Fair Zayda to her Zegri gave that token privily.
10085-8{_}2033{_}And ever as they rode, he looked upon his lady's boon.
10085-8{_}2034{_}"God knows," quoth he, "what fate may be--I may be slaughtered soon;   Thou still art mine, though scarce the sign of hope that bloomed whilere,   But in my grave I yet shall have my Zayda's token dear."
10085-8{_}2035{_}Young Lisaro was musing so, when onward on the path,   He well could see them riding slow; then pricked he in his wrath.
10085-8{_}2036{_}The raging sire, the kinsmen of Zayda's hateful house,   Fought well that day, yet in the fray the Zegri won his spouse.
10085-8{_}2037{_}THE BRIDAL OF ANDALLA  [The following ballad has been often imitated by modern poets, both in Spain and in Germany:    "_Pon te a las rejas azules, dexa la manga que labras,   Melancholica Xarifa, veras al galan Andalla."
10085-8{_}2038{_}etc_.]
10085-8{_}2039{_}"Rise up, rise up, Xarifa, lay the golden cushion down;   Rise up, come to the window, and gaze with all the town.
10085-8{_}2040{_}From gay guitar and violin the silver notes are flowing,   And the lovely lute doth speak between the trumpet's lordly blowing,   And banners bright from lattice light are waving everywhere,   And the tall, tall plume of our cousin's bridegroom floats proudly in the                 air:     Rise up, rise up, Xarifa, lay the golden cushion down;     Rise up, come to the window, and gaze with all the town.
10085-8{_}2041{_}"Arise, arise, Xarifa, I see Andalla's face,   He bends him to the people with a calm and princely grace.
10085-8{_}2042{_}Through all the land of Xeres and banks of Guadalquivir   Rode forth bridegroom so brave as he, so brave and lovely never.
10085-8{_}2043{_}Yon tall plume waving o'er his brow of purple mixed with white,   I guess 'twas wreathed by Zara, whom he will wed to-night;     Rise up, rise up, Xarifa, lay the golden cushion down;     Rise up, come to the window, and gaze with all the town.
10085-8{_}2044{_}"What aileth thee, Xarifa, what makes thine eyes look down?
10085-8{_}2045{_}Why stay ye from the window far, nor gaze with all the town?
10085-8{_}2046{_}I've heard you say on many a day, and sure you said the truth,   Andalla rides without a peer, among all Granada's youth.
10085-8{_}2047{_}Without a peer he rideth, and yon milk-white horse doth go   Beneath his stately master, with a stately step and slow;      Then rise, oh, rise, Xarifa, lay the golden cushion down;     Unseen here through the lattice, you may gaze with all       the town."
10085-8{_}2048{_}The Zegri lady rose not, nor laid her cushion down,   Nor came she to the window to gaze with all the town;   But though her eyes dwelt on her knee, in vain her fingers strove,   And though her needle pressed the silk, no flower Xarifa wove;   One bonny rose-bud she had traced, before the noise drew nigh--   That bonny bud a tear effaced, slow drooping from her eye.
10085-8{_}2049{_}"No--no," she sighs--"bid me not rise, nor lay my cushion down,     To gaze upon Andalla with all the gazing town."
10085-8{_}2050{_}"Why rise ye not, Xarifa, nor lay your cushion down?
10085-8{_}2051{_}Why gaze ye not, Xarifa, with all the gazing town?
10085-8{_}2052{_}Hear, hear the trumpet how it swells, and how the people cry!
10085-8{_}2053{_}He stops at Zara's palace gate--why sit ye still--oh, why?"
10085-8{_}2054{_}"At Zara's gate stops Zara's mate; in him shall I discover   The dark-eyed youth pledged me his truth with tears, and was my lover?
10085-8{_}2055{_}I will not rise, with dreary eyes, nor lay my cushion down,     To gaze on false Andalla with all the gazing town!"
10085-8{_}2056{_}ZARA'S EAR-RINGS    "My ear-rings!
10085-8{_}2057{_}my ear-rings!
10085-8{_}2058{_}they've dropped into the well,   And what to say to Muça, I cannot, cannot tell."
10085-8{_}2059{_}'Twas thus, Granada's fountain by, spoke Albuharez' daughter,   "The well is deep, far down they lie, beneath the cold blue water--   To me did Muça give them, when he spake his sad farewell,   And what to say when he comes back, alas!
10085-8{_}2060{_}I cannot tell.
10085-8{_}2061{_}"My ear-rings!
10085-8{_}2062{_}my ear-rings!
10085-8{_}2063{_}they were pearls in silver set,   That when my Moor was far away, I ne'er should him forget,   That I ne'er to other tongue should list, nor smile on other's tale,   But remember he my lips had kissed, pure as those ear-rings pale--   When he comes back, and hears that I have dropped them in the well,   Oh, what will Muça think of me, I cannot, cannot tell.
10085-8{_}2064{_}"My ear-rings!
10085-8{_}2065{_}my ear-rings!
10085-8{_}2066{_}he'll say they should have been,   Not of pearl and of silver, but of gold and glittering sheen,   Of jasper and of onyx, and of diamond shining clear,   Changing to the changing light, with radiance insincere--   That changeful mind unchanging gems are not befitting well--   Thus will he think--and what to say, alas!
10085-8{_}2067{_}I cannot tell.
10085-8{_}2068{_}"He'll think when I to market went, I loitered by the way;   He'll think a willing ear I lent to all the lads might say;   He'll think some other lover's hand, among my tresses noosed,   From the ears where he had placed them, my rings of pearl unloosed;   He'll think, when I was sporting so beside this marble well,   My pearls fell in,--and what to say, alas!
10085-8{_}2069{_}I cannot tell.
10085-8{_}2070{_}"He'll say, I am a woman, and we are all the same;   He'll say I loved when he was here to whisper of his flame--   But when he went to Tunis my virgin troth had broken,   And thought no more of Muça, and cared not for his token.
10085-8{_}2071{_}My ear-rings!
10085-8{_}2072{_}my ear-rings!
10085-8{_}2073{_}O luckless, luckless well,   For what to say to Muça, alas!
10085-8{_}2074{_}I cannot tell.
10085-8{_}2075{_}"I'll tell the truth to Muça, and I hope he will believe--   That I thought of him at morning, and thought of him at eve;   That, musing on my lover, when down the sun was gone,   His ear-rings in my hand I held, by the fountain all alone;   And that my mind was o'er the sea, when from my hand they fell,   And that deep his love lies in my heart, as they lie in the well."
10085-8{_}2076{_}THE LAMENTATION FOR CELIN    At the gate of old Granada, when all its bolts are barred,   At twilight at the Vega gate there is a trampling heard;   There is a trampling heard, as of horses treading slow,   And a weeping voice of women, and a heavy sound of woe.
10085-8{_}2077{_}"What tower is fallen, what star is set, what chief come these                 bewailing?"
10085-8{_}2078{_}"A tower is fallen, a star is set.
10085-8{_}2079{_}Alas!
10085-8{_}2080{_}alas for Celin!"
10085-8{_}2081{_}Three times they knock, three times they cry, and wide the doors they                 throw;   Dejectedly they enter, and mournfully they go;   In gloomy lines they mustering stand beneath the hollow porch,   Each horseman grasping in his hand a black and flaming torch;   Wet is each eye as they go by, and all around is wailing,   For all have heard the misery.
10085-8{_}2082{_}"Alas!
10085-8{_}2083{_}alas for Celin!"
10085-8{_}2084{_}--    Him yesterday a Moor did slay, of Bencerraje's blood,   'Twas at the solemn jousting, around the nobles stood;   The nobles of the land were by, and ladies bright and fair   Looked from their latticed windows, the haughty sight to share;   But now the nobles all lament, the ladies are bewailing,   For he was Granada's darling knight.
10085-8{_}2085{_}"Alas!
10085-8{_}2086{_}alas for Celin!"
10085-8{_}2087{_}Before him ride his vassals, in order two by two,   With ashes on their turbans spread, most pitiful to view;   Behind him his four sisters, each wrapped in sable veil,   Between the tambour's dismal strokes take up their doleful tale;   When stops the muffled drum, ye hear their brotherless bewailing,   And all the people, far and near, cry--"Alas!
10085-8{_}2088{_}alas for Celin!"
10085-8{_}2089{_}Oh!
10085-8{_}2090{_}lovely lies he on the bier, above the purple pall,   The flower of all Granada's youth, the loveliest of them all;   His dark, dark eyes are closed, his rosy lip is pale,   The crust of blood lies black and dim upon his burnished mail,   And evermore the hoarse tambour breaks in upon their wailing,   Its sound is like no earthly sound--"Alas!
10085-8{_}2091{_}alas for Celin!"
10085-8{_}2092{_}The Moorish maid at the lattice stands, the Moor stands at his door,   One maid is wringing of her hands, and one is weeping sore--   Down to the dust men bow their heads, and ashes black they strew   Upon their broidered garments of crimson, green, and blue--   Before each gate the bier stands still, then bursts the loud bewailing,   From door and lattice, high and low--"Alas!
10085-8{_}2093{_}alas for Celin!"
10085-8{_}2094{_}An old, old woman cometh forth, when she hears the people cry;   Her hair is white as silver, like horn her glazèd eye.
10085-8{_}2095{_}Twas she that nursed him at her breast, that nursed him long ago;   She knows not whom they all lament, but soon she well shall know.
10085-8{_}2096{_}With one deep shriek she thro' doth break, when her ears receive their                 wailing--   "Let me kiss my Celin ere I die--Alas!
10085-8{_}2097{_}alas for Celin!"
10085-8{_}2098{_}THE STORY OF SIDI BRAHIM OF MASSAT  [_Translated by Réne Basset and Chauncey C. Starkweather_]    THE STORY OF SIDI BRAHIM OF MASSAT  I  The Taleb Sidi Brahim, son of Amhammed of Massat, in the province of Sous, tells the following story about himself: When he was still a child at his father's house he went to the mosque to read with a taleb.
10085-8{_}2099{_}He studied with him for twelve and a half years.
10085-8{_}2100{_}His father gave him bread and kouskous, and he ate eight deniers' worth a day.
10085-8{_}2101{_}I will make known the country of Massat.
10085-8{_}2102{_}It contains seventeen towns.
10085-8{_}2103{_}In the middle of these is a market.
10085-8{_}2104{_}The Jews have a refuge in the village of the chief named Mobarek-ben-Mahomet.
10085-8{_}2105{_}He lives with a sheik called Brahim-Mahomet-Abon-Djemaa.
10085-8{_}2106{_}These two chiefs levy a tax on the Jews.
10085-8{_}2107{_}They receive from them four ounces per family at the beginning of each month.
10085-8{_}2108{_}If the festival of the Mussulmans coincides with the Sabbath of the Jews, the latter pay to each of the chiefs one ounce for a Jew or a Jewess, boy or girl, little or big.
10085-8{_}2109{_}The following are the details of the population of Massat.
10085-8{_}2110{_}It includes 1,700 men.
10085-8{_}2111{_}As to the women, little boys or girls, only the Lord knows their number.
10085-8{_}2112{_}There are 1,250 houses.
10085-8{_}2113{_}The horses amount to 180.
10085-8{_}2114{_}They ride them and make them work like oxen and mules.
10085-8{_}2115{_}They also fight on horseback.
10085-8{_}2116{_}The country has trees, vines, figs, cacti, dates, oranges, lemons, apples, apricots, melons, and olives.
10085-8{_}2117{_}There is a river which flows from there to the sea.
10085-8{_}2118{_}The commerce is considerable.
10085-8{_}2119{_}There are Jews and Mussulmans.
10085-8{_}2120{_}The number of books in the mosque is unknown, unless it be by God.
10085-8{_}2121{_}The teachers are numerous as well as the pilgrims, the descendants of Mahomet, and the saints.
10085-8{_}2122{_}May God aid us with his blessing!
10085-8{_}2123{_}We will now speak of the tribute which the people of Massat pay yearly to Prince Mouley-Abd-Er-Rahman.
10085-8{_}2124{_}Up to our days they had, for fifty-one years, given him 5,000 livres of silver.
10085-8{_}2125{_}The prince said to them, "You must pay 1,000 livres more."
10085-8{_}2126{_}They answered, "By the Lord, we will only give you as before, 5,000 livres, a slave, a servant, and a horse."
10085-8{_}2127{_}The kaid Abd-el-Cadik, who was caliph of the King of Taroundant, hastened to send against them forty-five horsemen, and said to them: "You must give me six thousand livres of silver, and a slave, a servant, and a horse in addition."
10085-8{_}2128{_}They refused and drove away the cavalry, saying, "Return to the kaid who sent you against us, and say to him that we will not increase our tribute as he demands."
10085-8{_}2129{_}The horsemen returned and arrived at Taroundant.
10085-8{_}2130{_}The kaid asked him, "Tell me what happened to you with the people of Massat."
10085-8{_}2131{_}They answered him, "They read in their assembly the letter that you sent them, and told us to go back, and that they would pay no larger sum."
10085-8{_}2132{_}The kaid called a council and asked what had better be done with the people of Massat.
10085-8{_}2133{_}The sheiks of the Achtouks answered, "Make complaints to the Sultan at Morocco."
10085-8{_}2134{_}He wrote to the Sultan, asking him to send an army to destroy the rebels of Massat.
10085-8{_}2135{_}The Sultan sent a force of 3,500 horsemen, to whom he gave for chief, Ettaib Eddin, who rejoined them near the khalifah of the King at Taroundant.
10085-8{_}2136{_}When the royal troops arrived, the fourth night, he started and led them to the taleb Mahomet of the Aggars, in the midst of the country of the Achtouks.
10085-8{_}2137{_}The taleb said to him: "Return to Taroundant.
10085-8{_}2138{_}Let your lieutenant go with them and we will talk about it."
10085-8{_}2139{_}The kaid answered, "Very well."
10085-8{_}2140{_}The chiefs of the Achtouks mounted their horses and led the army toward the country of Hama, in the mountain which is between the Achtouks and Ida-Oultit.
10085-8{_}2141{_}The troops hastened toward the foot of the mountain, near the river Alras, in the country of Takourt.
10085-8{_}2142{_}The mountaineers marched against them and fought for three days until the holy men and the sherifs arrived and quieted them.
10085-8{_}2143{_}The mountaineers came down toward the army.
10085-8{_}2144{_}The kaid betrayed them.
10085-8{_}2145{_}He seized fourteen of their leaders and sent them to the kaid at Taroundant.
10085-8{_}2146{_}He cut off their heads and hung them up at the gate.
10085-8{_}2147{_}As to the army that was above the river Alras, it attacked the people of Massat on account of the tribute demanded by the kaid.
10085-8{_}2148{_}It made the onset with cavalry, and destroyed the country.
10085-8{_}2149{_}The natives received them with powder, and they fought half a day.
10085-8{_}2150{_}The natives gained the advantage in the fight.
10085-8{_}2151{_}The enemy abandoned their cannons.
10085-8{_}2152{_}The natives slew them until the Sultan's troops retreated.
10085-8{_}2153{_}They captured 700 horses.
10085-8{_}2154{_}The troops of the Sultan abandoned their baggage except six chests of silver.
10085-8{_}2155{_}Many guns were broken on that day, until the flying invaders reached, the country of the Achtouks.
10085-8{_}2156{_}The people of Massat had for allies the tribes of Aglou and Tizpit, who equalled them in number.
10085-8{_}2157{_}As for the cannons abandoned the day of the battle, the conquerors took two of them to their country.
10085-8{_}2158{_}They kept them until they were repaid the 6,500 livres of silver, which had been taken from them.
10085-8{_}2159{_}Then they gave back the cannons.
10085-8{_}2160{_}Such is the complete story of that which happened between the tribe of Massat, the Khalifah of the King, and the neighboring tribes.
10085-8{_}2161{_}II  Information about the country of Tazroualt.
10085-8{_}2162{_}The Taleb Sidi Brahim, son of Mahomet, of Massat in Sous, tells the following: He started for the zaouiah of Tazroualt, to study there during seven months with the taleb Sidi Mahomet Adjeli, one of the greatest lights.
10085-8{_}2163{_}The number of students was seventy-four.
10085-8{_}2164{_}Forty-two of these studied the law.
10085-8{_}2165{_}The others read the Koran.
10085-8{_}2166{_}None of the students paid for his living.
10085-8{_}2167{_}It was furnished by the chief of the country, Hecham.
10085-8{_}2168{_}He gave to the zaouiah mentioned, six servants and six slaves to cook the food of the students.
10085-8{_}2169{_}The number of the villages of this country is nine.
10085-8{_}2170{_}The Kashlah of Hecham is situated in the middle of the country.
10085-8{_}2171{_}The Jewish quarter is at the left.
10085-8{_}2172{_}The market is held every day at the entrance to the fort.
10085-8{_}2173{_}This latter is built of stone, lime, and pine planks and beams.
10085-8{_}2174{_}Riches abound.
10085-8{_}2175{_}Caravans go from there to Timbuctoo, the Soudan, Sahara, and Agadir-Ndouma.
10085-8{_}2176{_}They go to these countries to buy ivory, ostrich feathers, slaves, gold and silver.
10085-8{_}2177{_}If it hurries, a caravan consumes a whole year in visiting these places.
10085-8{_}2178{_}The people of the different countries buy from them and give in exchange other merchandise, such as linen, cotton, silks, iron, steel, incense, corals, cloves, spikenard, haberdashery, pottery, glass, and everything that comes, as they say, from the country of Christians.
10085-8{_}2179{_}When these goods enumerated above have arrived, the merchants, both Jews and Mussulmans, come forward and buy them according to the needs of their business.
10085-8{_}2180{_}I will add here, with more details, some words about Hecham.
10085-8{_}2181{_}He has twelve sons, all horsemen, who have thirty-six horses.
10085-8{_}2182{_}As for oxen, sheep, and camels, God alone could tell the figure.
10085-8{_}2183{_}The number of the wives that Hecham has married is four white and six slaves--the latter black.
10085-8{_}2184{_}His only son has as many white wives as his father, but more black ones.
10085-8{_}2185{_}The men of Tizeroualt are of the number of 1,400.
10085-8{_}2186{_}But for the women, boys, and girls, God alone knows the figure.
10085-8{_}2187{_}They possess 200 horses, beside those of Hecham.
10085-8{_}2188{_}There are 750 houses; the number of books in the mosque is 130--in the Chelha language.
10085-8{_}2189{_}III  The sheik Sidi Hammad, son of Mahomet Mouley Ben-Nacer, has written his book in Amazir.
10085-8{_}2190{_}It is entitled the "Kitab-amazir."
10085-8{_}2191{_}This work treats of obligations and traditions of things permitted and forbidden.
10085-8{_}2192{_}IV  There are 3,500 men in the Aglou country.
10085-8{_}2193{_}They have 2,200 houses and 960 horses.
10085-8{_}2194{_}This district is on the sea-coast and possesses a stone-harbor.
10085-8{_}2195{_}There are barks which are used in fishing.
10085-8{_}2196{_}The inhabitants were living in tranquillity when one day, as they were starting out to fish, a ship arrived off shore.
10085-8{_}2197{_}They fled in fear and left it in the sea.
10085-8{_}2198{_}The ship waited till midnight.
10085-8{_}2199{_}Then it entered the port and ran up a red flag.
10085-8{_}2200{_}It remained at anchor for fifteen days.
10085-8{_}2201{_}The people of Aglou assembled day and night, big and little, even the horsemen before it.
10085-8{_}2202{_}No one was missing.
10085-8{_}2203{_}The chiefs of the town wrote letters which they sent to all the villages.
10085-8{_}2204{_}They sent one to Sidi Hecham couched in these words: "Come at once.
10085-8{_}2205{_}The Christians have made an expedition against us, and have taken this port."
10085-8{_}2206{_}Sidi Hecham sent messengers to all the provinces over which he ruled and said in his letters: "You must accompany me to the country of Aglou, for the Christians have made an expedition against us."
10085-8{_}2207{_}All the neighboring tribes assembled to march against the Christians.
10085-8{_}2208{_}When Sidi Hecham had joined them he said, "You must raise a red flag like theirs."
10085-8{_}2209{_}They raised it.
10085-8{_}2210{_}When it was seen by those on the ship, a sailor came ashore in a small boat and approached the Mussulmans there assembled.
10085-8{_}2211{_}"Let no one insult the Christian," said Sidi Hecham, "until we learn his purpose in landing here."
10085-8{_}2212{_}They asked him, "What do you want?"
10085-8{_}2213{_}The Christian replied, "We wish to receive, in the name of God, pledges of security."
10085-8{_}2214{_}All who were present said, "God grants to you security with us."
10085-8{_}2215{_}The Christian then continued, "My object is to trade with you."
10085-8{_}2216{_}"That is quite agreeable to us," answered Hecham.
10085-8{_}2217{_}Then Hecham asked the Christian what he wanted to purchase."
10085-8{_}2218{_}"Oil, butter, wheat, oxen, sheep, and chickens," said he.
10085-8{_}2219{_}When the Mussulmans heard this they gathered together wheat, oil, oxen, and everything he had mentioned.
10085-8{_}2220{_}He made his purchases, and was well supplied.
10085-8{_}2221{_}The master of the ship then said:  "Our business is finished.
10085-8{_}2222{_}We must go back home.
10085-8{_}2223{_}But we shall return to you."
10085-8{_}2224{_}Hecham answered:  "That which I have done for you is not pleasing to the people of Aglou.
10085-8{_}2225{_}It is only on account of the pledge of security that I have been able to restrain them.
10085-8{_}2226{_}I have given you all you asked.
10085-8{_}2227{_}Next time you come, bring us fifty cannons and ten howitzers."
10085-8{_}2228{_}"Very well," answered the Christian, "I shall return this time next year."
10085-8{_}2229{_}"Do as you promise," replied Hecham, "and I will give you whatever you want in the country of the Mussulmans."
10085-8{_}2230{_}V  A STORY ABOUT THE COUNTRY OF AIT-BAMOURAN  There arrived in this country at the beginning of the year another ship which stopped at a place called Ifni, in the tribe of Ait-Bamouran, and stayed there three days.
10085-8{_}2231{_}Then one of the sailors got into a small boat, came ashore, and said to the inhabitants, "I will buy bread, meat, and water from you."
10085-8{_}2232{_}The Mussulmans brought him bread, figs, and water, saying: "You must send two of your men ashore while we go on board the ship with you."
10085-8{_}2233{_}"It is well," replied the Christian.
10085-8{_}2234{_}Then he went to get two of his men whom he brought ashore and said to the Mussulmans: "You must give me one of your men."
10085-8{_}2235{_}They gave him a hostage to remain on board the Christian ship.
10085-8{_}2236{_}Then they filled a boat, and boarded the ship themselves to deliver what they had sold.
10085-8{_}2237{_}They ran all over the ship looking at everything.
10085-8{_}2238{_}Then they said, "Come with us to the spring and we will draw water."
10085-8{_}2239{_}The Christians accompanied them to the fountain to fill their water-casks.
10085-8{_}2240{_}The other natives, to the number of fifteen, got into a boat and went to the ship.
10085-8{_}2241{_}With the water-party and the hostages ashore there were only four Christians on the ship when the Mussulmans boarded it.
10085-8{_}2242{_}"Don't come aboard till our men have come back," said the Christians.
10085-8{_}2243{_}"We will come aboard by force," he was answered, and the attack began.
10085-8{_}2244{_}One of the Christians killed a native with a gun.
10085-8{_}2245{_}Then they fought until the Christians were overcome.
10085-8{_}2246{_}Two Christians were killed and the rest captured and taken ashore and imprisoned with the others of the water-party.
10085-8{_}2247{_}The ship was sold for 180 mithkals.
10085-8{_}2248{_}The Christians were all sold and dispersed among the tribes.
10085-8{_}2249{_}The news of this spread to Taccourt.
10085-8{_}2250{_}The merchants there sent to Ait-Bamouran and bought all the Christians at any price.
10085-8{_}2251{_}They secured seven.
10085-8{_}2252{_}Three were missing, of whom two were in the country of Ait-bou-Bekr with the chief of that tribe named Abd-Allah, son of Bou-Bekr.
10085-8{_}2253{_}The third, who was a boy, was with the sheik of Aglou, who said:  "I will not sell this one, for he has become as dear to me as a son."
10085-8{_}2254{_}Then addressing the young boy he said, "I wish to convert you; be a Mussulman."
10085-8{_}2255{_}The boy acquiesced and embraced Islamism.
10085-8{_}2256{_}The day of his abjuration the sheik killed in his honor an ox for a festival, and gave to the convert the name of Mahomet.
10085-8{_}2257{_}Then he sent to say to all his tribe:  "Come to my house.
10085-8{_}2258{_}I have prepared a repast."
10085-8{_}2259{_}The Mussulmans came and diverted themselves with their horses and gunpowder.
10085-8{_}2260{_}The chief told them, "I have given a fourth of my possessions, a slave, and a servant to this young man."
10085-8{_}2261{_}He added, "He shall live with my son."
10085-8{_}2262{_}They both occupied the same room, and the master taught the young convert the whole Koran.
10085-8{_}2263{_}The Mussulmans called him Sidi Mahomet, son of AH.
10085-8{_}2264{_}Seven Christians were ransomed and sent back to their own country.
10085-8{_}2265{_}VI  Information about the country Tiznit: This place is a kind of a city surrounded on all sides by a wall, and having only two gates.
10085-8{_}2266{_}The water is in the centre, in a fountain.
10085-8{_}2267{_}The fortress is built above the fountain, in the middle of the city.
10085-8{_}2268{_}It is entirely constructed of mortar, cut stone, marble, and beams, all from Christian countries.
10085-8{_}2269{_}It was the residence of the khalifah of the King in the time of Mouley-Soliman.
10085-8{_}2270{_}When this prince died, the people of Tiznit revolted, drove away the lieutenant, and made a concerted attack upon the citadel, which they completely destroyed.
10085-8{_}2271{_}They took the stones and beams and built a mosque on the spot, near the fountain of which we have spoken.
10085-8{_}2272{_}But when Mouley-Abd-Er-Rahman came to the throne he sent a caliph to Tiznit.
10085-8{_}2273{_}He gave him 300 horsemen.
10085-8{_}2274{_}When the caliph arrived near the town he waited three days and they gave him food and barley.
10085-8{_}2275{_}At the end of this time he made a proclamation summoning all the people to him.
10085-8{_}2276{_}When they came he read them the royal edict and said:  "I must enter your city to occupy the fortress of the King!"
10085-8{_}2277{_}They said: "No; go back whence you came and say to your master: 'You shall not rule over us.
10085-8{_}2278{_}Your fortress is totally destroyed, and with the material we have built a big mosque in the middle of our city.'"
10085-8{_}2279{_}Prince Mouley-Abd-Er-Rahman sent at once against them his son Sidi-Mahomet with the khalifah and 6,000 horsemen.
10085-8{_}2280{_}The people of Tiznit were informed of the approach of the army under the Sultan's son, and that the advancing guard was near.
10085-8{_}2281{_}The soldiers arrived in the middle of the country of the Achtouks and camped in the city of Tebouhonaikt near the river Alras.
10085-8{_}2282{_}There was a day's march between them and Tiznit.
10085-8{_}2283{_}The inhabitants, frightened, sent deputies to the other districts, saying:  "Come and help us, for the Sultan's son has come and ordered us to build him a fort in the space of one month or he will fall upon us, cut a passage, and destroy our city."
10085-8{_}2284{_}The tribes around Tiznit assembled and marched against the royal army.
10085-8{_}2285{_}The Sultan's son stayed twenty-two days at Tebouhonaikt, then he crossed the river Alras and marched against the rebels.
10085-8{_}2286{_}He surrounded Tiznit on all sides.
10085-8{_}2287{_}The inhabitants made a sortie, engaged in battle, and fought till the morning star.
10085-8{_}2288{_}At the fall of day the battle recommenced.
10085-8{_}2289{_}The royal army was defeated and driven across the river Alras.
10085-8{_}2290{_}The son of the Sultan killed eight rebels and thirty-five horses, but many of his soldiers fell.
10085-8{_}2291{_}He retreated to Morocco.
10085-8{_}2292{_}VII  Information about the country of Taragoust: This is a unique district situated near the source of the Ourd-Sous.
10085-8{_}2293{_}It is distant from Taroundant about a day and a half's march.
10085-8{_}2294{_}When a young man becomes of age his father buys him a gun and a sabre.
10085-8{_}2295{_}The market is in the middle of the country.
10085-8{_}2296{_}But no man goes there without his weapons.
10085-8{_}2297{_}The sheiks judge each one in the market for four months in the year in turn and during their period of office.
10085-8{_}2298{_}They decided who was guilty and demanded price of blood for those killed in the market.
10085-8{_}2299{_}One of them said:  "I will give nothing.
10085-8{_}2300{_}Find the murderer.
10085-8{_}2301{_}He will give you the price of blood."
10085-8{_}2302{_}The sheik replied: "Pay attention.
10085-8{_}2303{_}Give us part of your goods."
10085-8{_}2304{_}"I will give you nothing," he answered.
10085-8{_}2305{_}In this way they quarrelled, until they began fighting with guns.
10085-8{_}2306{_}Each tried to steal the other's horses and oxen in the night and kill the owner.
10085-8{_}2307{_}They kept acting this way toward each other until Ben-Nacer came to examine the villages where so many crimes were committed, and he reestablished peace and order.
10085-8{_}2308{_}VIII  Concerning guns and sabres: They were all brought into the city of Adjadir in the government of Sidi Mahomet-ben-Abd-Alla.
10085-8{_}2309{_}They introduced guns, poniards, sabres, English powder, and everything one can mention from the country of the Christians.
10085-8{_}2310{_}Sidi Mahomet-ben-Abd-Allah sent there his khalifah, called Ettaleb Calih.
10085-8{_}2311{_}He busied himself during his administration in amassing a great fortune.
10085-8{_}2312{_}The guns imported into the provinces were called merchandise of the taleb Calih.
10085-8{_}2313{_}This officer revolted against the Sultan, sent him no more money, and consulted him no longer in the administration of affairs.
10085-8{_}2314{_}When the prince ordered him to do such and such a thing with the Christians, Mussulmans, or others, he replied:  "I shall do as I please, for all the people of Sous are under my hand.
10085-8{_}2315{_}I leave the rest to you."
10085-8{_}2316{_}The Sultan sent much money to Sidi Mahomet-ben-Abd-Allah, and ordered him with troops against the rebel.
10085-8{_}2317{_}The latter fought against the divan until he was captured and put in fetters and chains.
10085-8{_}2318{_}The partisans of the Emperor said to him:  "We have captured your khalifah Ettaleb Calih and his accomplices."
10085-8{_}2319{_}The prince responded: "Make him a bonnet of iron and a shirt of iron, and give him but a loaf of bread a day."
10085-8{_}2320{_}In a letter that he sent he said also:  "Collect all the goods you can find and let the Christian ships take them all to Taccourt, leaving nothing whatever."
10085-8{_}2321{_}Guns, sabres, powder, sulphur, linens, cottons, everything was transported.
10085-8{_}2322{_}During the reign of Sidi Mouley Soliman he built the city as it is at present.
10085-8{_}2323{_}He increased it, and said to the Christians:  "You must bring me cannons, mortars, and powder, and I will give you in exchange wheat, oil, wool, and whatever you desire."
10085-8{_}2324{_}The Christians answered: "Most willingly, we shall return with our products."
10085-8{_}2325{_}They brought him cannons, mortars, and powder.
10085-8{_}2326{_}In return he supplied them with woollens, wheat, oil, and whatever they desired.
10085-8{_}2327{_}The Ulmas reproached him, saying: "You are not fulfilling the law in giving to the Christians wheat, oil, and woollens.
10085-8{_}2328{_}You are weakening the Mussulmans."
10085-8{_}2329{_}He answered them: "We must make sacrifices of these goods for two or three years, until the Christians have stocked us with cannons, powder, and so forth.
10085-8{_}2330{_}These I will place in the coast towns to drive off the infidels when they arrive."
10085-8{_}2331{_}IX  More words about guns: They only make them in three cities in the interior of Sous.
10085-8{_}2332{_}The workmen are very numerous.
10085-8{_}2333{_}They make also gun-barrels, pistols, gun-locks, and all such things.
10085-8{_}2334{_}As for sabres and poniards, they are made by Arab armorers.
10085-8{_}2335{_}They make powder in every province, but only in small quantities.
10085-8{_}2336{_}FIVE BERBER STORIES  [_Translated by G. Mercier and Chauncey C. Starkweather_]    DJOKHRANE AND THE JAYS  The ancestor of the grandfather of Mahomet Amokrane was named Djokhrane.
10085-8{_}2337{_}He was a Roman of old times, who lived at T'kout at the period of the Romans.
10085-8{_}2338{_}One of his countrymen rose against them, and they fought.
10085-8{_}2339{_}This Roman had the advantage, until a bird of the kind called jays came to the assistance of Djokhrane, and pecked the Roman in the eyes until he saved his adversary.
10085-8{_}2340{_}From that time forth he remained a friend to Djokhrane.
10085-8{_}2341{_}The latter said to his children:  "As long as you live, never eat this bird.
10085-8{_}2342{_}If you meet anyone who brings one of these birds to eat, buy it and set it free."
10085-8{_}2343{_}To this day when anyone brings a jay to one of his descendants, he buys it for silver and gives it liberty.
10085-8{_}2344{_}This story is true, and is not a lie.
10085-8{_}2345{_}THE OGRE AND THE BEAUTIFUL WOMAN  Some hunters set out with their camels.
10085-8{_}2346{_}When they came to the hunting-ground they loosed their camels to let them graze, and hunted until the setting of the sun, and then came back to their camp.
10085-8{_}2347{_}One day while one of them was going along he saw the marks of an ogre, each one three feet wide, and began to follow them.
10085-8{_}2348{_}He proceeded and found the place where the ogre had lately made his lair.
10085-8{_}2349{_}He returned and said to his companions:  "I've found the traces of an ogre.
10085-8{_}2350{_}Come, let us seek him."
10085-8{_}2351{_}"No," they answered, "we will not go to seek him, because we are not stronger than he is."
10085-8{_}2352{_}"Grant me fourteen days," said the huntsman.
10085-8{_}2353{_}"If I return, you shall see.
10085-8{_}2354{_}If not, take back my camel with the game."
10085-8{_}2355{_}The next day he set out and began to follow the traces of the ogre.
10085-8{_}2356{_}He walked for four days, when he discovered a cave, into which he entered.
10085-8{_}2357{_}Within he found a beautiful woman, who said to him:  "What brings thee here, where thou wilt be devoured by this ogre?"
10085-8{_}2358{_}"But thou," answered the hunter, "what is thy story and how did the ogre bring thee here?"
10085-8{_}2359{_}"Three days ago he stole me," she replied.
10085-8{_}2360{_}"I was betrothed to the son of my uncle, then the ogre took me.
10085-8{_}2361{_}I have stayed in the cavern.
10085-8{_}2362{_}He often brings me food.
10085-8{_}2363{_}I stay here, and he does not kill me."
10085-8{_}2364{_}"Where does he enter," asked the hunter, "when he comes back here?"
10085-8{_}2365{_}"This is the way," she answered.
10085-8{_}2366{_}The hunter went in to the middle of the cave, loaded his gun, and waited.
10085-8{_}2367{_}At sunset the ogre arrived.
10085-8{_}2368{_}The hunter took aim and fired, hitting the ogre between the eyes as he was sitting down.
10085-8{_}2369{_}Approaching him he saw that he had brought with him two men to cook and eat them.
10085-8{_}2370{_}In the morning he employed the day in collecting the hidden silver, took what he could, and set out on the return.
10085-8{_}2371{_}On the fourteenth day he arrived at the place where he had left his comrades, and found them there.
10085-8{_}2372{_}"Leave the game you have secured and return with me to the cave," he said to them.
10085-8{_}2373{_}When they arrived they took all the arms and clothing, loaded it upon their camels, and set out to return to their village.
10085-8{_}2374{_}Half way home they fought to see which one should marry the woman.
10085-8{_}2375{_}The powder spoke between them.
10085-8{_}2376{_}Our man killed four, and took the woman home and married her.
10085-8{_}2377{_}THE FALSE VEZIR  A king had a wife who said to him: "I would like to go and visit my father."
10085-8{_}2378{_}"Very well," said he; "wait to-day, and to-morrow thou shalt go with my vezir."
10085-8{_}2379{_}The next day they set out, taking the children with them, and an escort lest they should be attacked on the way.
10085-8{_}2380{_}They stopped at sunset, and passed the night on the road.
10085-8{_}2381{_}The vezir said to the guards, "Watch that we be not taken, if the robbers should come to seize us."
10085-8{_}2382{_}They guarded the tent.
10085-8{_}2383{_}The vezir asked the King's wife to marry him, and killed one of her sons because she refused.
10085-8{_}2384{_}The next day they set out again.
10085-8{_}2385{_}The next night he again asked the King's wife to marry him, threatening to kill a second child should she refuse.
10085-8{_}2386{_}She did refuse, so he killed the second son.
10085-8{_}2387{_}The next morning they set out, and when they stopped at night again he asked the King's wife to marry him.
10085-8{_}2388{_}"I'll kill you if you refuse."
10085-8{_}2389{_}She asked for delay, time to say her prayers.
10085-8{_}2390{_}She prayed to God, the Master of all worlds, and said: "O God, save me from the vezir."
10085-8{_}2391{_}The Master of the worlds heard her prayer.
10085-8{_}2392{_}He gave her the wings of a bird, and she flew up in the sky.
10085-8{_}2393{_}At dawn she alighted in a great city, and met a man upon the roadside.
10085-8{_}2394{_}She said: "By the face of God, give me your raiment and I'll give thee mine."
10085-8{_}2395{_}"Take it, and may God honor you," he said.
10085-8{_}2396{_}Then she was handsome.
10085-8{_}2397{_}This city had no king.
10085-8{_}2398{_}The members of the council said:  "This creature is handsome; we'll make him our king."
10085-8{_}2399{_}The cannon spoke in his honor and the drums beat.
10085-8{_}2400{_}When she flew up into the sky, the vezir said to the guards: "You will be my witnesses that she has gone to the sky, so that when I shall see the King he cannot say, 'Where is she?'"
10085-8{_}2401{_}But when the vezir told this story, the King said:  "I shall go to seek my wife.
10085-8{_}2402{_}Thou hast lied.
10085-8{_}2403{_}Thou shalt accompany me."
10085-8{_}2404{_}They set out, and went from village to village.
10085-8{_}2405{_}They inquired, and said: "Has a woman been found here recently?
10085-8{_}2406{_}We have lost her."
10085-8{_}2407{_}And the village people said, "We have not found her."
10085-8{_}2408{_}They went then to another village and inquired.
10085-8{_}2409{_}At this village the Sultan's wife recognized them, called her servant, and said to him, "Go, bring to me this man."
10085-8{_}2410{_}She said to the King, "From what motive hast thou come hither?"
10085-8{_}2411{_}He said, "I have lost my wife."
10085-8{_}2412{_}She answered: "Stay here, and pass the night.
10085-8{_}2413{_}We will give thee a dinner and will question thee."
10085-8{_}2414{_}When the sun had set she said to the servant, "Go, bring the dinner, that the guests may eat."
10085-8{_}2415{_}When they had eaten she said to the King, "Tell me your story."
10085-8{_}2416{_}He answered: "My story is long.
10085-8{_}2417{_}My wife went away in the company of a trusted vezir.
10085-8{_}2418{_}He returned and said: 'By God, your wife has gone to heaven.'
10085-8{_}2419{_}"I replied: 'No, you have lied.
10085-8{_}2420{_}I'll go and look for her.'"
10085-8{_}2421{_}She said to him, "I am your wife."
10085-8{_}2422{_}"How came you here?"
10085-8{_}2423{_}he asked.
10085-8{_}2424{_}She replied: "After having started, your vezir came to me and asked me to marry him or he would kill my son, 'Kill him,' I said, and he killed them both."
10085-8{_}2425{_}Addressing the vezir, she said: "And your story?
10085-8{_}2426{_}Let us hear it."
10085-8{_}2427{_}"I will return in a moment," said the vezir, for he feared her.
10085-8{_}2428{_}But the King cut off his head.
10085-8{_}2429{_}The next day he assembled the council of the village, and his wife said, "Forgive me and let me go, for I am a woman."
10085-8{_}2430{_}THE SOUFI AND THE TARGUI  Two Souafa were brothers.
10085-8{_}2431{_}Separating one day one said to the other: "O my brother, let us marry thy son with my daughter."
10085-8{_}2432{_}So the young cousins were married, and the young man's father gave them a separate house.
10085-8{_}2433{_}It happened that a man among the Touareg heard tell of her as a remarkable woman.
10085-8{_}2434{_}He mounted his swiftest camel, ten years old, and went to her house.
10085-8{_}2435{_}Arrived near her residence, he found some shepherds.
10085-8{_}2436{_}"Who are you?"
10085-8{_}2437{_}he said.
10085-8{_}2438{_}"We are Souafa."
10085-8{_}2439{_}He confided in one of them, and said to him: "By the face of the Master of the worlds, O favorite of fair women, man of remarkable appearance, tell me if the lady so and so, daughter of so and so, is here."
10085-8{_}2440{_}"She is here."
10085-8{_}2441{_}"Well, if you have the sentiments of most men, I desire you to bring her here, I want to see her."
10085-8{_}2442{_}"I will do what you ask.
10085-8{_}2443{_}If she'll come, I'll bring her.
10085-8{_}2444{_}If not, I will return and tell you."
10085-8{_}2445{_}He set out, and, arriving at the house of the lady, he saw some people, and said "Good-evening" to them.
10085-8{_}2446{_}"Come dine with us," they said to him.
10085-8{_}2447{_}"I have but just now eaten and am not hungry."
10085-8{_}2448{_}He pretended to amuse himself with them to shorten the night, in reality to put to sleep their vigilance.
10085-8{_}2449{_}These people went away to amuse themselves while he met the lady.
10085-8{_}2450{_}"A man sends me to you," he said, "a Targui, who wants to marry you.
10085-8{_}2451{_}He is as handsome as you are, his eyes are fine, his nose is fine, his mouth is fine."
10085-8{_}2452{_}"Well, I will marry him."
10085-8{_}2453{_}She went to him and married him, and they set out on a camel together.
10085-8{_}2454{_}When the first husband returned, he found that she had gone.
10085-8{_}2455{_}He said to himself: "She is at my father's or perhaps my uncle's."
10085-8{_}2456{_}When day dawned he said to his sister, "Go see if she is in thy father's house or thy uncle's."
10085-8{_}2457{_}She went, and did not find her there.
10085-8{_}2458{_}He went out to look for her, and perceived the camel's traces.
10085-8{_}2459{_}Then he saddled his own camel.
10085-8{_}2460{_}The women came out and said: "Stay!
10085-8{_}2461{_}Do not go; we will give thee our own daughters to marry."
10085-8{_}2462{_}"No," he replied, "I want to find my wife."
10085-8{_}2463{_}He goes out, he follows the tracks of the camel, here, here, here, until the sun goes down.
10085-8{_}2464{_}He spends the night upon the trail.
10085-8{_}2465{_}His camel is a runner of five years.
10085-8{_}2466{_}When the sun rises he starts and follows the trail again.
10085-8{_}2467{_}About four o'clock he arrives at an encampment of the Touareg, and finds some shepherds with their flocks.
10085-8{_}2468{_}He confides in one of these men, and says to him: "A word, brave man, brother of beautiful women, I would say a word to thee which thou wilt not repeat."
10085-8{_}2469{_}"Speak."
10085-8{_}2470{_}"Did a woman arrive at this place night before last?"
10085-8{_}2471{_}"She did."
10085-8{_}2472{_}"Hast thou the sentiments of a man of heart?"
10085-8{_}2473{_}"Truly."
10085-8{_}2474{_}"I desire to talk to her."
10085-8{_}2475{_}"I will take thee to her.
10085-8{_}2476{_}Go, hide thy camel; tie him up.
10085-8{_}2477{_}Change thy clothing.
10085-8{_}2478{_}Thou wilt not then be recognized among the sheep.
10085-8{_}2479{_}Bring thy sabre and come.
10085-8{_}2480{_}Thou shalt walk as the sheep walk."
10085-8{_}2481{_}"I will walk toward you, taking the appearance of a sheep, so as not to be perceived."
10085-8{_}2482{_}"The wedding-festival is set for to-night, and everybody will be out of their houses.
10085-8{_}2483{_}When I arrive at the tent of this lady I will strike a stake with my stick.
10085-8{_}2484{_}Where I shall strike, that is where she lives."
10085-8{_}2485{_}He waits and conceals himself among the flocks, and the women come out to milk.
10085-8{_}2486{_}He looks among the groups of tents.
10085-8{_}2487{_}He finds his wife and bids her come with him.
10085-8{_}2488{_}"I will not go with thee, but if thou art hungry, I will give thee food."
10085-8{_}2489{_}"Thou'lt come with me or I will kill thee!"
10085-8{_}2490{_}She goes with him.
10085-8{_}2491{_}He finds his camel, unfastens him, dons his ordinary clothing, takes his wife upon the camel's back with him, and departs.
10085-8{_}2492{_}The day dawns.
10085-8{_}2493{_}She says:  "O thou who art the son of my paternal uncle, I am thirsty."
10085-8{_}2494{_}Now she planned a treachery.
10085-8{_}2495{_}He said to her: "Is there any water here?"
10085-8{_}2496{_}"The day the Targui took me off we found some in that pass."
10085-8{_}2497{_}They arrived at the well.
10085-8{_}2498{_}"Go down into the well," said the Soufi.
10085-8{_}2499{_}"I'm only a woman.
10085-8{_}2500{_}I'm afraid.
10085-8{_}2501{_}Go down thyself."
10085-8{_}2502{_}He goes down.
10085-8{_}2503{_}He draws the water.
10085-8{_}2504{_}She drinks.
10085-8{_}2505{_}He draws more water for the camel, which is drinking, when she pours the water on the ground.
10085-8{_}2506{_}"Why dost thou turn out the water?"
10085-8{_}2507{_}"I did not turn it out; thy camel drank it."
10085-8{_}2508{_}And nevertheless she casts her glances and sees a dust in the distance.
10085-8{_}2509{_}The Targui is coming.
10085-8{_}2510{_}The woman says:  "Now I have trapped him for thee."
10085-8{_}2511{_}"Brava!"
10085-8{_}2512{_}he cries, and addressing the Soufi: "Draw me some water that I may drink."
10085-8{_}2513{_}He draws the water, and the Targui drinks.
10085-8{_}2514{_}The woman says to him: "Kill him in the well.
10085-8{_}2515{_}He is a good shot.
10085-8{_}2516{_}Thou art not stronger than he is."
10085-8{_}2517{_}"No," he answered, "I do not want to soil a well of the tribes.
10085-8{_}2518{_}I'll make him come up."
10085-8{_}2519{_}The Soufi comes up till his shoulders appear.
10085-8{_}2520{_}They seize him, hoist and bind him, and tie his feet together.
10085-8{_}2521{_}Then they seize and kill his camel.
10085-8{_}2522{_}"Bring wood," says the Targui to the woman; "we'll roast some meat."
10085-8{_}2523{_}She brings him some wood.
10085-8{_}2524{_}He cooked the meat and ate it, while she roasted pieces of fat till they dripped upon her cousin.
10085-8{_}2525{_}"Don't do that," says the Targui.
10085-8{_}2526{_}She says, "He drew his sword on me, crying, 'Come with me or I will kill thee.'"
10085-8{_}2527{_}"In that case do as you like."
10085-8{_}2528{_}She dropped the grease upon his breast, face, and neck until his skin was burnt.
10085-8{_}2529{_}While she was doing this, the Targui felt sleep coming upon him, and said to the woman, "Watch over him, lest he should slip out of our hands."
10085-8{_}2530{_}While he slept the Soufi speaks: "Word of goodness, O excellent woman, bend over me that I may kiss thy mouth or else thy cheek."
10085-8{_}2531{_}She says: "God make thy tent empty.
10085-8{_}2532{_}Thou'lt die soon, and thou thinkest of kisses?"
10085-8{_}2533{_}"Truly I am going to die, and I die for thee.
10085-8{_}2534{_}I love thee more than the whole world.
10085-8{_}2535{_}Let me kiss thee once.
10085-8{_}2536{_}I'll have a moment of joy, and then I'll die."
10085-8{_}2537{_}She bends over him, and he kisses her.
10085-8{_}2538{_}She says, "What dost thou want?"
10085-8{_}2539{_}"That thou shalt untie me."
10085-8{_}2540{_}She unties him.
10085-8{_}2541{_}He says to her: "Keep silent.
10085-8{_}2542{_}Do not speak a word."
10085-8{_}2543{_}Then he unfastens the shackles that bind his feet, puts on his cloak, takes his gun, draws out the old charge and loads it anew, examines the flint-lock and sees that it works well.
10085-8{_}2544{_}Then he says to the woman, "Lift up the Targui."
10085-8{_}2545{_}The latter awakes.
10085-8{_}2546{_}"Why," says he, "didst thou not kill me in my sleep?"
10085-8{_}2547{_}"Because thou didst not kill me when I was in the well.
10085-8{_}2548{_}Get up.
10085-8{_}2549{_}Stand down there, while I stand here."
10085-8{_}2550{_}The Targui obeys, and says to the Soufi: "Fire first."
10085-8{_}2551{_}"No, I'll let thee fire first."
10085-8{_}2552{_}The woman speaks: "Strike, strike, O Targui, thou art not as strong as the Soufi."
10085-8{_}2553{_}The Targui rises, fires, and now the woman gives voice to a long "you--you."
10085-8{_}2554{_}It strikes the _chechias_ that fly above his head.
10085-8{_}2555{_}At his turn the Soufi prepares himself and says:  "Stand up straight now, as I did for thee."
10085-8{_}2556{_}He fires, and hits him on the forehead.
10085-8{_}2557{_}His enemy dead, he flies at him and cuts his throat.
10085-8{_}2558{_}He then goes to the camel, cuts some meat, and says to the woman: "Go, find me some wood, I want to cook and eat."
10085-8{_}2559{_}"I will not go," she says.
10085-8{_}2560{_}He approaches, threatening her, and strikes her.
10085-8{_}2561{_}She gets up then and brings him some wood.
10085-8{_}2562{_}He cooks the meat and eats his fill.
10085-8{_}2563{_}He thinks then of killing the woman, but he fears that the people of his tribe will say, "Thou didst not bring her back."
10085-8{_}2564{_}So he takes her on the camel and starts homeward.
10085-8{_}2565{_}His cousins are pasturing their flocks on a hill.
10085-8{_}2566{_}When he had nearly arrived a dust arose.
10085-8{_}2567{_}He draws near, and they see that it is he.
10085-8{_}2568{_}His brother speaks, "What have they done to thee?"
10085-8{_}2569{_}He answers, "The daughter of my uncle did all this."
10085-8{_}2570{_}Then they killed the woman and cut her flesh in strips and threw it on a jujube-tree.
10085-8{_}2571{_}And the jackals and birds of prey came and passed the whole day eating it, until there was none left.
10085-8{_}2572{_}AHMED EL HILALIEU AND EL REDAH  Ahmed el Hilalieu was not loved by people in general.
10085-8{_}2573{_}His enemies went and found an old sorceress, and spoke to her as follows: "O sorceress, we want you to drive this man out of our country.
10085-8{_}2574{_}Ask what you will, we will give it to you!"
10085-8{_}2575{_}She said to them: "May God gladden your faces.
10085-8{_}2576{_}Call aloud.
10085-8{_}2577{_}Our man will come out and I will see him."
10085-8{_}2578{_}They obeyed her, crying out that a camel had escaped.
10085-8{_}2579{_}Straightway Ahmed goes to find his father, and tells him his intention of going to join in the search.
10085-8{_}2580{_}He starts forth mounted on his courser, and on the way meets some people, who tell him, "It is nothing."
10085-8{_}2581{_}He makes a half turn, not forgetting to water his horse, and meets at the fountain the sorceress, who was drawing water.
10085-8{_}2582{_}"Let me pass," he said to her, "and take your buckskin out of my way."
10085-8{_}2583{_}"You may pass," she answered.
10085-8{_}2584{_}He started his horse, which stepped on the buckskin and tore it.
10085-8{_}2585{_}"You who are so brave with a poor woman," she said, "would you be able to bring back Redah Oum Zaid?"
10085-8{_}2586{_}"By the religion of Him whom I adore, you shall show me where this Redah lives or I'll cut off your head."
10085-8{_}2587{_}"Know, then, that she lives far from here, and that there is between her and you no less than forty days' journey."
10085-8{_}2588{_}Ahmed went home, and took as provisions for the journey forty dates of the deglet-nour variety, putting them into his pocket.
10085-8{_}2589{_}He mounted his steed and departed.
10085-8{_}2590{_}He goes and goes without stopping, until he comes to the country of the sand.
10085-8{_}2591{_}The charger throws his feet forward and buries himself in the sand up to his breast, but soon stops, conquered and worn out by fatigue.
10085-8{_}2592{_}Ahmed el Hilalieu then addresses him:    "My good gray horse, of noble mien, the sand,   The cruel sand would eat your very eyes.
10085-8{_}2593{_}The air no longer thy loud whinnies bears,   No strength is left thee in thy head or heart.
10085-8{_}2594{_}The prairies of Khafour I'll give to thee,   With Nouna's eyes I'll quench thy thirst, by God   A mule's whole pack of barley shalt thou have   That Ben Haddjouna shall bring here for thee."
10085-8{_}2595{_}In his turn the steed spoke and said: "Dismount, unfasten the breast-strap, tighten the girth, for some women are coming to show themselves to us in this country."
10085-8{_}2596{_}Ahmed unfastened the breast-strap, then remounts and departs.
10085-8{_}2597{_}While he proceeds he sees before him the encampment of a tribe, and perceives a horseman coming, mounted on a white mare, engaged in herding camels.
10085-8{_}2598{_}"Blessings upon you!"
10085-8{_}2599{_}cried Ahmed; "you behind the camels!"
10085-8{_}2600{_}The horseman kept silence, and would not return his salutations.
10085-8{_}2601{_}"Greetings to you," cried Ahmed again, "you who are in the middle of the camels."
10085-8{_}2602{_}The same obstinate silence.
10085-8{_}2603{_}"Greetings to you, you who are before the camels."
10085-8{_}2604{_}The horseman still was silent.
10085-8{_}2605{_}Ahmed then said: "Greetings to you, you who own the white mare."
10085-8{_}2606{_}"Greetings to you!"
10085-8{_}2607{_}replied the horseman.
10085-8{_}2608{_}"How comes it that you would not answer my greetings for so long?"
10085-8{_}2609{_}The horseman answered: "You cried to me, 'Greetings to you, you who are behind the camels,' Now, behind them are their tails.
10085-8{_}2610{_}Then you said, 'Greetings to you, you who are in the middle of the camels,' In the middle of them are their bellies.
10085-8{_}2611{_}You said, again, 'Greetings to you, you who are before the camels.'
10085-8{_}2612{_}Before them are their heads.
10085-8{_}2613{_}You said, 'Greetings to you, O master of the white mare,' And then I answered to you, 'Greetings to you also,'"  Ahmed el Hilalieu asked of the shepherd, "What is your name?"
10085-8{_}2614{_}"I am called Chira."
10085-8{_}2615{_}"Well, Chira, tell me where Redah lives.
10085-8{_}2616{_}Is it at the city of the stones or in the garden of the palms?"
10085-8{_}2617{_}"Redah dwells in the city.
10085-8{_}2618{_}Her father is the Sultan.
10085-8{_}2619{_}Seven kings have fought for her, and one of them has refreshed his heart.
10085-8{_}2620{_}He is named Chalau.
10085-8{_}2621{_}Go, seek the large house.
10085-8{_}2622{_}You will be with Redah when I see you again."
10085-8{_}2623{_}Ahmed sets out, and soon meets the wife of the shepherd, who comes before him and says, "Enter, be welcome, and may good luck attend you!"
10085-8{_}2624{_}She ties his horse, gives him to drink, and goes to find dates for Ahmed.
10085-8{_}2625{_}She takes care to count them before serving him with them.
10085-8{_}2626{_}He takes out a pit, closes the date again, puts them all together, and puts down the pit.
10085-8{_}2627{_}He ate nothing, and he said to the woman: "Take away these dates, for I have eaten my fill."
10085-8{_}2628{_}She looks, takes up the tray, counts the dates again, and perceives that none of them has been eaten.
10085-8{_}2629{_}Nevertheless, there is a pit, and not a date missing.
10085-8{_}2630{_}She cries out:    "Alas!
10085-8{_}2631{_}my heart for love of this young man   Is void of life as is this date of pit."
10085-8{_}2632{_}Then she heaved a sigh and her soul flew away.
10085-8{_}2633{_}Ahmed remained there as if in a dream until the shepherd came back.
10085-8{_}2634{_}"Your wife is dead," he said to him, "and if you wish, I'll give you her weight in gold and silver."
10085-8{_}2635{_}But the shepherd answers: "I, too, am the son of a sultan.
10085-8{_}2636{_}I have come to pay this woman a visit and desire to see her.
10085-8{_}2637{_}Calm yourself.
10085-8{_}2638{_}I will take neither your gold nor silver.
10085-8{_}2639{_}This is the road to follow; go, till you arrive at the castle where she is."
10085-8{_}2640{_}Ahmed starts, and when he arrives at the castle, he stands up in his stirrups and throws the shadow of his spear upon the window.
10085-8{_}2641{_}Redah, addressing her negress, said to her: "See now what casts that shadow.
10085-8{_}2642{_}Is it a cloud, or an Arab's spear?"
10085-8{_}2643{_}The negress goes to see, comes back to her mistress, and says to her, "It is a horseman, such as I have never seen the like of before in all my life."
10085-8{_}2644{_}"Return," said Redah, "and ask him who he is."
10085-8{_}2645{_}Redah goes to see, and says:    "O horseman, who dost come before our eyes,   Why seekest thou thy death?
10085-8{_}2646{_}Tell me upon   Thine honor true, what is thine origin?"
10085-8{_}2647{_}He answers:    "Oh, I am Ahmed el Hilalieu called.
10085-8{_}2648{_}Well known   'Mongst all the tribes of daughters of Hilal.
10085-8{_}2649{_}I bear in hand a spear that loves to kill,   Who'er attacks me counts on flight and dies."
10085-8{_}2650{_}She says to him:    "Thou'rt Ahmed el Hilalieu?
10085-8{_}2651{_}Never prowls   A noble bird about the Zeriba;   The generous falcon turns not near the nests,   O madman!
10085-8{_}2652{_}Why take so much care   About a tree that bears not any dates?"
10085-8{_}2653{_}He answers:    "I will demand of our great Lord of all   To give us rain to cover all the land   With pasturage and flowers.
10085-8{_}2654{_}And we shall eat   Of every sort of fruit that grows on earth."
10085-8{_}2655{_}Redah:    "We women are like silk.
10085-8{_}2656{_}And only those   Who are true merchants know to handle us."
10085-8{_}2657{_}Ahmed el Hilalieu then says:    "I've those worth more than thou amid the girls   Of Hilal, clad in daintiest of silk   Of richest dye, O Redah, O fifth rite."
10085-8{_}2658{_}And, turning his horse's head, he goes away.
10085-8{_}2659{_}But she recalls him:    "I am an orange, them the gardener;   I am a palm and thou dost cut my fruit;   I am a beast and thou dost slaughter me.
10085-8{_}2660{_}I am--upon thine honor--O gray steed,   Turn back thy head.
10085-8{_}2661{_}For we are friends henceforth."
10085-8{_}2662{_}She says to the negress, "Go open wide the door that he may come."
10085-8{_}2663{_}The negress admits him, and ties up his horse.
10085-8{_}2664{_}On the third day he sees the negress laughing.
10085-8{_}2665{_}"Why do you laugh, negress?"
10085-8{_}2666{_}"You have not said your prayers for three days."
10085-8{_}2667{_}POEMS OF THE MAGHREB  [_Translated by M.C.
10085-8{_}2668{_}Sonneck and Chauncey C. Starkweather_]    ALI'S ANSWER  [ARGUMENT.--It is related that a young man named Aly ben Bou Fayd, falling in love with a young woman, begged his father to ask her in marriage for him.
10085-8{_}2669{_}His father refused.
10085-8{_}2670{_}Angered, Aly procured a gun, engraved his name upon it, and betook himself to the chase.
10085-8{_}2671{_}His father having claimed this gun he answered:]    You ask the gun I have that bears my name.
10085-8{_}2672{_}I will not give it, save against my will.
10085-8{_}2673{_}How comes it, father, that you treat me thus?
10085-8{_}2674{_}You say, "Bring back the gun to put in pledge."
10085-8{_}2675{_}Now, may God pardon you for acting thus!
10085-8{_}2676{_}I leave you in your land, and, all for you,   I swear by God I never shall return.
10085-8{_}2677{_}Your conduct is unwise.
10085-8{_}2678{_}Our enemies   Insult me, O my father.
10085-8{_}2679{_}And I think   That you will give up your ancestral home   And garden too.
10085-8{_}2680{_}And can I after that   Recover my good gun?
10085-8{_}2681{_}I shall not be   Enfeebled that I am no more with you.
10085-8{_}2682{_}No longer are you father unto me,   And I shall be no more your cherished son.
10085-8{_}2683{_}I think, my sire, that you are growing old.
10085-8{_}2684{_}Your teeth are falling out from day to day.
10085-8{_}2685{_}They whom you visit will not serve you more.
10085-8{_}2686{_}Your friends won't serve you longer, and your sire,   He who begot you, will not help you now.
10085-8{_}2687{_}In your adversity no help will come   From all your kindred's high nobility.
10085-8{_}2688{_}May God make easy all the paths you tread!
10085-8{_}2689{_}His uncle having threatened him with death, he answered:    Keep far away from him who has not come   To thee in his misfortune.
10085-8{_}2690{_}Leave him free.
10085-8{_}2691{_}My uncle writes to me this very day   That if he held in his own hands the leaf   Of my life's destiny he'd blot it out.
10085-8{_}2692{_}If he had in his hands this leaf, O say to him:    Let him efface it openly, nor hide   You'll not be able, save with God's own help   To bear the separation.
10085-8{_}2693{_}As for those   Who are so evil, we will spare them now.
10085-8{_}2694{_}The barrel of this gun is rusted red.
10085-8{_}2695{_}The lock is forceless, 'twill no longer act.
10085-8{_}2696{_}Misfortune overtake the man who leaves   His child to perish!
10085-8{_}2697{_}For the least of things   He says to me, "Come, give me up this gun."
10085-8{_}2698{_}I go to seek the desert.
10085-8{_}2699{_}I will go   Among the tribe they call Oulad Azyz,   And live by force.
10085-8{_}2700{_}But, pray you say to her,   The fair one with the deftly braided hair,   I leave the tribe, but shall return for her.
10085-8{_}2701{_}I disappear, but shall come back for her.
10085-8{_}2702{_}And while I live, I never shall forget.
10085-8{_}2703{_}I swear it by the head of that sweet one   Who for the sake of Ali was accused.
10085-8{_}2704{_}The cup of passion which I offered her   O'ercame her lovely spirit's tenderness.
10085-8{_}2705{_}The cup of love intoxicated her.
10085-8{_}2706{_}O God, Creator of us all, give her   The strength to bear my absence!
10085-8{_}2707{_}Sad for me   The hour I dream of her I love so well.
10085-8{_}2708{_}Her love is in my heart and burns it up.
10085-8{_}2709{_}My heart is sad.
10085-8{_}2710{_}'Tis love that crushes it.
10085-8{_}2711{_}It leaves my heart reduced to naught but dust.
10085-8{_}2712{_}So that I am consumed by vigils long,   And never taste refreshing sleep at all.
10085-8{_}2713{_}So that I'm like a bird with broken wings,    Just like a bird who tries to lift its wings!
10085-8{_}2714{_}And so my spirit is not healed.
10085-8{_}2715{_}There comes   To me no comfort nor relief.
10085-8{_}2716{_}The eyes   Of my beloved are as bright as day.
10085-8{_}2717{_}One word from her would send the friends to death.
10085-8{_}2718{_}IN HONOR OF LALLA AYCHA-EL-MANNOUBYYA    A fire burns at the bottom of my heart,   For love has conquered me, and I am now   His hostage and his prisoner.
10085-8{_}2719{_}My soul   Is torn out from my body, and sweet sleep   Keeps far aloof from my tired eyelids' need.
10085-8{_}2720{_}'Tis Aycha causes this, the pretty one.
10085-8{_}2721{_}With blackest eyes, Aycha the pure, from whom   I'm parted now, whose name is finest gold.
10085-8{_}2722{_}Why?
10085-8{_}2723{_}why?
10085-8{_}2724{_}Oh, tell me, El Mannoubyya.
10085-8{_}2725{_}Why all this coldness, O my best beloved?
10085-8{_}2726{_}For thy dear love I have drunk deep of scorn.
10085-8{_}2727{_}For thy love, maiden with the darksome looks,   I wither while thou bear'st a port of oak.
10085-8{_}2728{_}The fire that burns me eats my very soul.
10085-8{_}2729{_}My spirit is distracted by these proofs.
10085-8{_}2730{_}O thou, rebellious to my warm desires,   My black-eyed beauty, if thou'rt vexed with me   I'll make apology before the world,   I'll bring an offering to thee at once,   The symbol of my homage.
10085-8{_}2731{_}May it please!
10085-8{_}2732{_}Instruct me, sympathetic with my pain   Have you not said: "I'll bring thee soon good news"?
10085-8{_}2733{_}O come!
10085-8{_}2734{_}That in my sleep my eyes may see   Thee coming toward me, my black-pupilled one!
10085-8{_}2735{_}Awaiting thy fair image I'm consumed,   I am exhausted.
10085-8{_}2736{_}Why, El Mannoubyya?
10085-8{_}2737{_}I long have hoped to see thee, O my sweet.
10085-8{_}2738{_}And ever farther off appears the end   Of my awaiting.
10085-8{_}2739{_}All my nights are passed   In cries for thee, as some poor mariner   Cries to the angry floods that dash aloft.
10085-8{_}2740{_}For thee I'm mad with love, my pretty one,   Struck with thy mien so full of nobleness.
10085-8{_}2741{_}And I alone must wither, 'mongst my friends.
10085-8{_}2742{_}O unpersuadable, with teasing eyes,   I am in a most pitiable state.
10085-8{_}2743{_}Since thou repell'st me and declin'st to keep   Thy promise to me, I'll not hesitate   To call thee before God.
10085-8{_}2744{_}Unless thou deign'st   To cast thy looks on me the coming day,   I shall, all clad in vestments rich, make plaint   Unto the envoy of our God, the last   Of all the prophets.
10085-8{_}2745{_}For thou said'st to me,   "I'll draw thee from the sea of thy despair."
10085-8{_}2746{_}I worship at thy sanctuary, sweet,   My beauty, with large eyes of darkest night.
10085-8{_}2747{_}Why?
10085-8{_}2748{_}why?
10085-8{_}2749{_}El Mannoubyya, tell me why.
10085-8{_}2750{_}Let thyself bend and call thy servitor,   Inhabitant of Tunis--city green.
10085-8{_}2751{_}I will apologize and come to thee,   O cruel one, with heavy frontlets dark.
10085-8{_}2752{_}We've heard the story of thy deeds so fine.
10085-8{_}2753{_}From common brass whene'er thou walk'st abroad,   Thou drawest silver pure, queen of thy time,   'Mongst men illumined by thy piety.
10085-8{_}2754{_}The wretch, led on by love, accosted thee.
10085-8{_}2755{_}Receiving grace, despite his base design   He was, nathless, forgiven and saved from sin;   So was it from eternity decreed.
10085-8{_}2756{_}They all consulted thee, queen of thy day,   And thou didst answer: "This man truly loved.
10085-8{_}2757{_}Pour him a cup of wine."
10085-8{_}2758{_}By thee he came   Unto perfection's acme, step by step.
10085-8{_}2759{_}Our Lord, all-powerful, gave to thee this power.
10085-8{_}2760{_}These are thy merits, fairest citizen!
10085-8{_}2761{_}To whom God gave strength irresistible.
10085-8{_}2762{_}O beauty with enchanting eyes, Aycha,   Our queen.
10085-8{_}2763{_}Si Alimed Khoudja, greatest bard   Of all that time, has said: "I wrote these words   The year one thousand one hundred just,   But thou who read'st these lines, where'er it be,   Add to these numbers, after ninety-eight."
10085-8{_}2764{_}Now I salute all those united here   And him who hates me here I steep in scorn.
10085-8{_}2765{_}Why?
10085-8{_}2766{_}why?
10085-8{_}2767{_}El Mannoubyya!
10085-8{_}2768{_}Why?
10085-8{_}2769{_}SAYD AND HYZYYA    Give me your consolation, noble friends;   The queen of beauties sleeps within the tomb.
10085-8{_}2770{_}A burning fire consumes my aching breast;   I am undone.
10085-8{_}2771{_}Alas!
10085-8{_}2772{_}O cruel fate!
10085-8{_}2773{_}My heart's with slim Hyzyya in the grave.
10085-8{_}2774{_}Alas!
10085-8{_}2775{_}we were so happy a short while   Ago, just like the prairie flow'rs in spring;   How sweet to us was life in those dear days!
10085-8{_}2776{_}Now like a phantom's shadow she has gone,   That young gazelle, of utter loveliness.
10085-8{_}2777{_}Removed by stern, inevitable fate.
10085-8{_}2778{_}When she walked forth, not looking right or left,   My beauteous loved one rendered fools the wise.
10085-8{_}2779{_}Impressed thus was the great bey of the camp.
10085-8{_}2780{_}A gleaming poniard rested in his belt.
10085-8{_}2781{_}He went hemmed in by soldiers and a horde   Of horsemen, glad to follow where he led.
10085-8{_}2782{_}All haste to bring him costly gifts.
10085-8{_}2783{_}He bore   A sabre of the Ind, and with one stroke   He cleaved a bar of iron, split a rock.
10085-8{_}2784{_}How many rebels fell beneath his blow!
10085-8{_}2785{_}Haughty and proud, he challenged all who came.
10085-8{_}2786{_}Enough now we have glorified the bey.
10085-8{_}2787{_}Speak, singer, in a song that's sweet and new,   The praises of the dainty girl I loved,   The daughter of good Ahmed ben el Bey.
10085-8{_}2788{_}Give me your consolation, noble friends;   The queen of beauties sleeps within the tomb.
10085-8{_}2789{_}A burning fire consumes my aching breast;   I am undone!
10085-8{_}2790{_}Alas!
10085-8{_}2791{_}O cruel fate!
10085-8{_}2792{_}She lets her tresses flow in all the breeze,   Exhaling sweet perfume.
10085-8{_}2793{_}Thy brows are arched   In beauty's curve.
10085-8{_}2794{_}Thy glance is like a ball   Shot from a Christian's gun, which hits the mark.
10085-8{_}2795{_}Thy cheek is lovely as the morning rose   Or bright carnation, and thy ruby blood   Gives it the shining brightness of the sun.
10085-8{_}2796{_}Thy teeth are ivory-white, and thy warm kiss   Is sweet as milk or honey loved by all.
10085-8{_}2797{_}Oh, see that neck, more white than palm-tree's heart,   That sheath of crystal, bound with bands of gold.
10085-8{_}2798{_}Thy chest is marble, and thy tender breasts   Are apples whose sweet scent makes well the ill.   Thy body is, like paper, shining, white,   Or cotton or fine linen, or, again,   Just like the snow that falls in a dark night.
10085-8{_}2799{_}Hyzyya lets her sash hang gracefully,   Down-falling to the earth, in fold on fold.
10085-8{_}2800{_}Her fine limbs jingle with gems she wears.
10085-8{_}2801{_}Her slippers clink with coupled rings of gold.
10085-8{_}2802{_}We were encamped at Bazer.
10085-8{_}2803{_}Every day   At dawn I saw the beauty, and we were   So glad together!
10085-8{_}2804{_}Every dawn I brought   My wishes to my love and followed fate   More happy than if I alone possessed   All riches and all treasures of the earth.
10085-8{_}2805{_}Wealth equals not the tinkle of her gems.
10085-8{_}2806{_}When I had crossed the mountain there I met   Hyzyya, and she walked amid the fields   With every grace, and made her bracelets ring.
10085-8{_}2807{_}My reason wandered, heart and head were vexed.
10085-8{_}2808{_}After a happy summer passed at Tell,   We came, my dearest one and I, Sahara-ward.
10085-8{_}2809{_}The litters now are closed, the powder sounds.
10085-8{_}2810{_}My gray horse to Hyzyya bears me swift.
10085-8{_}2811{_}The palanquin of my coquette's on route.
10085-8{_}2812{_}At Azal when night comes we pitch our tents.
10085-8{_}2813{_}Sydy-l-Ahsen is before us now:   Ez-Zerga, too.
10085-8{_}2814{_}Then faring on we go   To Sydy Sayd, and Elmetkeouk,   And Medoukal-of-palms, where we arrive   At eventide.
10085-8{_}2815{_}We saddle up at dawn,   Just when the breeze begins.
10085-8{_}2816{_}Our halting-place,   Sydy Mehammed, decks this peaceful earth.
10085-8{_}2817{_}From there the litters seek El Mekheraf.
10085-8{_}2818{_}My charger gray straight as an eagle goes.
10085-8{_}2819{_}I wend to Ben Seryer with my love,   Of tattooed arms.
10085-8{_}2820{_}When we had crossed Djedy   We passed the wide plain, and we spent the night   At Rous-et-toual, near the gleaming sands.
10085-8{_}2821{_}Ben Djellal was our next day's resting-place;   And, leaving there, I camped at El Besbas,   And last at El-Herymek, with my love.
10085-8{_}2822{_}How many festivals beheld us then!
10085-8{_}2823{_}In the arena my good steed of gray   Fled like a ghost.
10085-8{_}2824{_}And sweet Hyzyya there,   Tall as a flagstaff, bent her gaze on me,   Her smile disclosing teeth of purest pearl.
10085-8{_}2825{_}She spoke but in allusions, causing thus   That I should understand whate'er she meant.
10085-8{_}2826{_}Hamyda's daughter then might be compared   Unto the morning-star or a tall palm,   Alone, erect among the other trees.
10085-8{_}2827{_}The wind uprooted it, and dashed it down.
10085-8{_}2828{_}I did not look to see it fall, this tree   I hoped forever to protect.
10085-8{_}2829{_}I thought   That God, divinely good, would let it live.
10085-8{_}2830{_}But God, the Master, dashed it to the earth.
10085-8{_}2831{_}I take up now my song.
10085-8{_}2832{_}We made but one   Encampment, at Oned Itel.
10085-8{_}2833{_}'Twas there   My friend, the queen of damsels, said farewell.
10085-8{_}2834{_}'Twas in the night she paid the debt of death.
10085-8{_}2835{_}'Twas there my dark-eyed beauty passed away.
10085-8{_}2836{_}She pressed her heart to mine and, sighing, died.
10085-8{_}2837{_}My cheeks were flooded with a sea of tears.
10085-8{_}2838{_}I thought to lose my reason.
10085-8{_}2839{_}I went forth   And wandered through the fields, ravines, and hills.
10085-8{_}2840{_}She bore my soul away, my black-eyed love.
10085-8{_}2841{_}The daughter of a noble race.
10085-8{_}2842{_}Alas!
10085-8{_}2843{_}She still increased the burnings of my heart.
10085-8{_}2844{_}They wrapped her in a shroud, my noble love.
10085-8{_}2845{_}The fever took me, burning up my brain.
10085-8{_}2846{_}They placed her on a bier, all decked with gems.
10085-8{_}2847{_}And I was in a stupor, dull to see   All that was passing on that dreadful day.
10085-8{_}2848{_}They bore my beauty in a palanquin--   Her pretty palanquin--this lovely girl,   Cause of my sorrows, tall as a straight staff.
10085-8{_}2849{_}Her litter is adorned with odd designs,   Shining as brilliant as the morning-star,   And like the rainbow glowing 'midst the clouds,   All hung with silk and figured damask-cloth.
10085-8{_}2850{_}And I, like any child, was in despair,   Mourning Hyzyya.
10085-8{_}2851{_}Oh, what pangs I felt   For her whose profile was so pure!
10085-8{_}2852{_}She nevermore   Will reappear upon this earth again.
10085-8{_}2853{_}She died the death of martyrs, my sweet love,   My fair'st one, with Koheul-tinted lids!
10085-8{_}2854{_}They took her to a country that is called   Sydy Kaled, and buried her at night,   My tattooed beauty.
10085-8{_}2855{_}And her lovely eyes,   Like a gazelle's, have never left my sight.
10085-8{_}2856{_}O sexton, care now for my sweet gazelle,   And let no stones fall on Hyzyya's grave.
10085-8{_}2857{_}I do adjure thee by the Holy Book   And by the letters which make up the name   Of God, the Giver of all good, let no   Earth fall upon the dame with mirror decked.
10085-8{_}2858{_}Were it to claim her from a rival's arms   I would attack three troops of warriors.
10085-8{_}2859{_}I'd take her from a hostile tribe by force.
10085-8{_}2860{_}Could I but swear by her dear head, my love,   My black-eyed beauty--I would never count   My enemies, 'though they a hundred were.
10085-8{_}2861{_}Were she unto the strongest to belong   I swear she never would be swept from me.
10085-8{_}2862{_}In the sweet name Hyzyya I'd attack   And fight with cavaliers innumerable.
10085-8{_}2863{_}Were she to be the spoil of conqueror,   You'd hear abroad the tale of my exploits.
10085-8{_}2864{_}I'd take her by main strength from all who vied.
10085-8{_}2865{_}Were she the meed of furious encounters   I'd fight for years for her, and win at last!
10085-8{_}2866{_}For I am brave.
10085-8{_}2867{_}But since it is the will   Of God, the mighty and compassionate,   I cannot ward away from me this blow.
10085-8{_}2868{_}I'll wait in patience for the happy day   When I shall join thee.
10085-8{_}2869{_}For I only think   Of thee, my dearest love, of thee alone!
10085-8{_}2870{_}My gray steed fell dead as he leaped.
10085-8{_}2871{_}O friends,   After my love, he's gone and left me, too.
10085-8{_}2872{_}My charger, 'mid these hills, was of all steeds   The fleetest, and in fiercest war's attack   All saw him at the head of the platoon.
